PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research t horoughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods hr which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, tram
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladde
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment il
the water, accompanied by slight burning 01
smarting sensation. Headaches, Drowsiness during

in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,

weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure

cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,

I Dr. M. PEEHT,
M.K.C.S., L.S.A , &c.,
SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical Board of N.S. W. Late Boon Sur-
geon to the Great Northern Hospital. London
1 am aware that I am not atrictlv following the paths
of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the treatment of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Phrenic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph, Nor.
6th 7th and Bth, 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater Importance, their health Is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified Men entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, vis., Advertising.
It is utterly Impossible for those who are suffering
from these diseases, who are scattered over all parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike n blow at the Quackery which now exists, but I alto add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
Mr Qualifications I Invite the fullest investigation into. For some time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of obsento t the many phases of the Nervous and

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
“ The Bulletin ” will return all unavailable MSS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
■uch MSS.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings

ran have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.

city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, ivith whom we do not ivish
to interfere.

SATURDAY, MAY 16, 1891.
Nullification.
The Bulletin, in discussing the results of
the Federal Conference held in Melbourne
in 1890, urged the Australias to “go slow’' in
their progress toward the goal of Federation.
In almost every reference that it has since
made to the problem this paper has repeated
the counsel. It repeats it again. Apart
from the personal vanity and unscrupulous-
ness of certain delegates to the late Conven-
tion, there is absolutely no earthly reason
why the educative process should not be
continued until that underlying sympathy
and that essential mutuality of interest, which,
quite apart from politicians and so-called
political ties, now identitiy the several
“colonies” as being distinctly one in
character and one in aspiration, expresses
itself in an unmistakable mandate to the Par-
liaments of the Provinces to establish a
Federal Government. No such mandate has
yet been issued by the people of any single
province. The delegation to the Federal
Convention was in no sense a popular one
in, or with respect to, any one province, and,
in at least three of the most important
“ colonies,” was the product of iniquitous
cabals. The petty tricks with which certain
hucksters of political wares purchased their
seats in the Convention have, in these
“ colonies,” not been ratified by the popular
voice or conscience. Neither have the
people of other “colonies ” declared them-
selves definitely in favour of the Federal
principle. The question has never been
made a test at any general election ; it has
not been granted a supreme position in any
Ministerial programme ; in no case have the
electors of any province had the opportunity
to declare for or against Federation.
* '* *
The assertion that the Draft Constitution
Bill will be the creation of the people of
Australia, in the event of its adoption
by a number of Parliaments, would thus
be the idlest nonsense were it not of
the most mischievous tendency. In every
country and in every age, Authority
has been created by a class or classes by
clothing large and unjustifiable pretensions
with the sanctities of constitutional procedure,
and a congeries of democratic countries here
are not better protected from the danger of
having their liberties whittled away by such
delusiv o steps than are oligarchically-ruled
nations. In the former, as in the latter,
“ ther pried of liberty is eternal vigilance,”
and in the former, as in the latter, the con-
sciousness of the people, sent to slumber by
the assurance that their interests are safe in
the hands of their constitutionally-appointed
i epi esentatives, may but have been drugged
to its own death. More treasons against the
commonweal in England have been per-
petrated with the sanction of constitutional
toims than were over perpetrated in defiance
of them. Our would-be masters here have
not failed to learn the lesson. The one way
to show them that they have learnt it in
vain is for Australia to declare that, in this
matter of Federation, it is not going to be
hurried, but that it intends to 11 go slow.”
Tim declaration may hurt some politicians’
feelings, but it may be called for the more
urgently for that very reason.
# # #
The Australias should proceed slowly if
they wish to proceed surely. “ The Federal
Union,” says Paekes, “is a wedding of
States, and the experience of the world of
politics no more than that of the world of
domesticity fails to encourage indulgence
in the belief that a hasty marriage will not
be followed by a leisured repentance. The
marriage will be attended by more auspicious
auguries when it is preceded by the fullest
discussion of the new responsibilities and
by education for the new duties which will be
obligated upon its consummation. “If
there is to be no ultimate separation, let the
steps towards union be well and carefully
measured,” is surely an expression of the
commonest prudence. It is an unfortunate
accident of the situation that it is not also
an expression of our highest “ colonial ”
statesmanship. The leading “ Statesmen ”
of the several States are, with such gifts of
gab and lung as lies within them, urging an
immediate union. But tlm circumstance
that they are going in bald-headed into
the business is precisely why the several
peoples should refuse to go in blindfolded.
The Australian intellect is seldom doing
better work than when it is distrusting the
politicians.
* * *
If the judgment of our “statesmen” as
declared in the “Draft of a Bill to Consti-
tute the Commonwealth of Australia ” be
accepted, tacitly or directly, by the people of
the several provinces, then will the Aus-
tralias be committed to a Federal Union that
must, inevitably, sooner or later, be
violently ruptured. The Federal Union, if
constituted, should be regarded as indis-
soluble. From the moment the States
enter into the compact to erect a Federal
Government, should date an immortal
vitality. New interests will be then created
which ought to be as lasting as any human
institution ; new commercial, social and
political relations will be established which,
humanly speaking, should be perpetual. But
is this immortal structure to be built hastily,
without the fullest possible gauging of
the future, without calling to the aid of
the builders that subtle instinct of the
mass, that “ common sense of most,” that
popular sanction, without which no political
institution can stand ? The politicians say
Yes ; the laws of permanence say u No ”
emphatically “No !” The Constitution of
the Lnited States of America was the pro-
duct not alone of a few weeks’ deliberations,
but of ten years’ experience of a loose con-
federacy. It was, as framed, the most magni-
ficent monument of political wisdom then ex-
tant, yet it was not perfect, for it distinctly
evaded, and carefully excluded from sight, a
difficulty that was even then more moral
than political. W hat was the result ?
Because of that evasion, distinctly and
designedly made, within a century the Union
had to fight for its existence and to drench
its ai eas with blood. Now, if without any
previous experience of confederacy, without
the same informative and educative exigency
of circumstance which infused the Fathers of
the American Union, without the material
pressure of the British bayonet-tips, which,
though peace had been declared, were still
palpable to the American sight, without
cither the like purity of patriotism or the
like degree of proved political intelligence ;
if without these things, but with like diffi-
culties, of a nature at once moral and political,
facingthem with only a desire to evade them, our
Conventionists produce a measure that is
neither as consistent with itself nor as lumi-
nous in its insight as the American, what
then? Can w T e, in Australia, hope to escape
the visitation of civil war, of rebellion, of
the bloody coercion by the more populous
and financially powerful States of the less-
populated and weaker ones ? If every
confederacy known to history, from the
Achrean and Uycian Leagues to the Ameri-
can, the Swiss, and the Canadian, has had to
threaten if not to exert the force of the cen-
tral or the major power upon the individual
States, it is insane for the Australian people
to refuse to believe in the possibility of a
rupture of the union. And, in the nature of
the case, the process of disunion—the seces-
sion of a State or States from the whole-
can never be a peaceable operation. When
a State proceeds to nullify an act of the Federal
Power, the nullification will mean war.
* * *
The Draft Constitution forbids nullifica-
tion. Wo have already, in those columns,
pointed out that chapter V., clause 0 :
" hen a law of a State is inconsistent with a law of the
Commonwealth, the latter shall prevail, and the former
shall, to the extent of the inconsistency, be invalid ;
embodies an insidious attack on State-rights.
In ninety-nine cases out of a hundred°this
clause will operate without any particular
injustice to the individual States, for in those
instances the point of “ inconsistency ” will
almost assuredly be of a nature in which
the matter involved is of a peculiarly
Australian importance as distinguished
from a merely State concern, and one
in which the good sense of the State’s
people will thus keep step with Australian
opinion. But in the hundredth case, say,
the dispute will turn upon a question which
both the Central Government and the State
will regard as essential and vital—the former
with relation to the interests of Australia as
a whole, the latter with respect to its effect
upon its own citizens. Which shall then
prevail—the Commonwealth or the State ?
Obviously the Constitutionalist will be com-
pelled to reply: “The Commonwealth.”
Equally obvious is it that the thick-and-thin
advocates of State Sovereignty and State
Rights will answer; “The State.” And
what then ? Coercion more or less dis-
guised—which it will require more con-
summate statesmanship than the Australian
politician, as we know him to-day, possesses,
to prevent developing into war. Peaceable
secession will be impossible, because, even
if secession be permissible on an equitable
adjuscmentof responsibilities entered into on
a State’s behalf by the Central Government, a
cool and rational settlement could not be
arrived at in the excited condition of feeling
which would necessarily be consequent upon
any conflict of the Federal and Provincial
powers. The State would feel oppressed
and would resent what it deemed to be an
a f?g ress ion j the Federal Executive would
have no option but to enforce the law by the
military arm. Is such a hundredth case im-
possible ? Let us state a hypothetical
difficulty which will only require the assumed
conditions, which must arise, to transform
the hypothesis into fact. It is only a ques-
tion of decades, perhaps of years, for
Northern Queensland to be erected into a
separate colony. Ask any North Queens-
lander, one who knows the country and
knows the forces at work or lying dormant
within it, and he will tell you that a
Northern Queensland legislature will enact
laws providing for the introduction of
coloured labour. Now, a Federal Govern-
ment dominated by the States in which the
agricultural, industrial and pastoral condi-
tions can be fed always and in every way by
white labour, will never legalise any such
measure. Here, then, we would have at
once a casus belli. Complicate the situa-
tion by establishing what must come, a
separate State in what is now called the
Northern Territory of South Australia, and
a third, and perhaps a fourth, State
in the tropical portions of Western
Australia, and there will be created
throughout those Northern States a
community of feeling and an identity of
interest diametrically opposed to the moral
sense and the political notiousof the Southern
States of the Union. We should have a
Mason and Dixey line in Australia, with its
inevitable resultants : the declaration by the
Federal Parliament that the States’ coloured
labour laws were “inconsistentthe passing
by the States’ legislature of acts rendering
“null and void” within their respective
boundaries the anti-coloured-labour acts of
the Federal Parliament ; secession ; con-
federacy between the North ; and between
the North and South—war. And—let this
be marked well —war in which the advantage
at the start will be with the Federal
Government, as the Draft Constitution
destroys the militia power of each State.
* * #
Conventionists of the Parkes - Munro
brand—the imbecilities of the Convention—
will dismiss this picture with a breath. From
Griffith we expect better things, and we
want to know what he says to it. He, of all
men, is aware that there is no falsity in the
perspective. He cannot fail to apprehend
that the North Queenslander, in defying the
power of the Federal Government, will
believe he is protecting, not alone the inter-
ests of his own pocket, but his dearest
liberty—the right to rule in those affairs that
concern himself. What does Griffith say ?
What doeslNGLis Clark, of Tasmania, say to
this t That in the conflict the Constitution
must prevail—that is what they will say. But
what will have become of the liberties of the
citizen of Northern Queensland which they
now declare are conserved under the Con-
stitution ? To deny the powers of nullifica-
tion to the individual States is to invite
secession and civil war ; to grant it, is to
render, on our hypothesis (which must
arrive), permanent union impossible. There
is a dilemma, but it is not of our making— at
is the creation of the Constitution-moulders.
And yet, to the present time, not a word
has been dropped by any Conventionist
that would indicate he has any sense of the
danger, the measureless danger, which lurks
within the clause we have quoted. There is
one way to avoid the danger. Crush the
dilemma. That can be done by informing
every elector of the Australias what he has
to expect when he casts his vote for the Con-
stitution. If he, voluntarily and consciously
realising the possibilities, abandons his nul-
lifying power, well and good—he acts within
his right and exercises his right. But let
him not be trapped by an insidiously-worded
clause into assenting to a Constitution which
deprives him of a liberty he now enjoys-
the right to legislate for his dearest interests.
What in the case supposed (which we have
purposely taken because The Bulletin’s
sympathies would be with respect to it on
the side of the Constitution) would be the
position of the North Queenslander —may, iu
other cases, be that of the citizen of every
other State. With that possibility before
the Australias, let them “go slow.”
* * #
“ The Southern States,” said Rufus
King, of Massachusetts, one of the
ablest, most temperate, and keenest-
sighted members of the Philadelphia Con-
vention of 1787, “ threaten to separate now
(from the Confederacy) in case injury should
be done them. There will be no point of
time at which they will not be able to say :
‘Do us Justice or we will separate. ’ ” King,
next to Franklin, the cleverest mouthpiece
of the most potent Northern States, placated
the Southern feeling, which even then was
prophetic of civil disaster, by these words.
But because the man who used them, and
his colleagues who applauded them, shut
their eyes to plain facts, such a noint of time
did arise. The Southern States, in 1861,
demanded what they regarded as justice.
With the exception of Bright, there was
scarcely a prominent Englishman who did
not conceive they rightly made the claim ;
yet it was refused, and they separated. The
world knows the issue. Let us, here in
Australia, not refuse to perceive the signifi-
cance of the way in which history falsified
King’s noteworthy assertion. Let us take a
full, steady view of facts and possibilities.
A Brutal Nation.
A few months ago the Pall Mall Gazette
started an enquiry under this heading, “ Are we
a Brutal Nation?” and after much investigation
and considerable raking up of mouldered
precedents and ancient information, it
resolved the question in the affirmative. Its
opinion was based on a long list of horrors—on
murders and premeditated homicides, and
sudden deaths—on that state of savagery which
relieves its overcharged soul by drunkenness
and wife-beating, and brutal assault—on that
strange, unfathomed instinct which leads the
lower-class Englishman to heave a half brick at
any stranger who comes in his way, and then to
kick him on the head—on prize-fighting, dog-
fighting, cock-fighting, horse-racing, pigeon-
shooting, hunting, and similar sports, and many
other evidences of a like nature. It considered
that joyous sentiment deeply rooted in the
soul of the cultured Briton, which makes him
find his chief pleasure and satisfaction in going
out with a gun and killing something—anything,
everything—he is not particular what it is so
long as he can make some miserable animal die
in torment ere giving it away; and which
enables him to snatch a fearful joy out of the
agonies of some wretched quadruped that is
being eaten alive by his dogs after being hunted
into the last stage of exhaustion. It pointed
out how the Anglo-Saxon cheerfully planks
down his last cent to see two illiterate char-
acters bang each other’s heads into an unplea-
sant mash, like a clubbed reptile ; and in view
of these and many similar characteristics which
mark that cultured islander wherever he goes,
it returned a verdict that the Briton in all his
home and foreign varieties is a brute. Probably
the verdict was correct, and yet the enquiry
hardly touched the beginning of the great
question, and it altogether omitted the most
reliable evidence of all. The Briton is brutal,
and coarse, and unfeeling in many respects, but
he is brutal chiefly in his utter inability to
understand his own deficiences. He has a
criminal code which is about as humane as the
average, but his administration of it is appalling
in its infamous pigheadedness, and callous, un~
feeling stupidity; and the difficulty seems to be
past remedy, for he is as incapable of learning
how to use his code in a reasonably sane fashion
as a hog is of learning how to use a telescope.
The telescope may be a fine instrument, but
with nothing but a hog to put at the end of it
it is useless. And the Anglo-Saxon criminal
law is useless for exactly the same reason—
there is a hog at the end of it also.
* # #
When a case of revolting cruelty is brought
before the law in any part of the British
Empire the hopeless inability of the Briton to
understand even the meaning of cruelty becomes
apparent. In Melbourne, a few days ago, a
free and enlightened citizen named Killin went
home and jumped on his wife with such vigour
that she only missed death by a hair’s-breadth,
and whether she will ever fully recover is a
matter of doubt. When he was interrupted in
his joyous performance his house resembled a
shambles and it was with difficulty that sundry
meddlesome and interfering inhabitants dragged
out the unrecognisable mass of blood and hair
and mangled humanity on top of which Killin
was bounding and gambolling. On this occa-
sion the law woke up with unwonted vigour
and energy, and the language of the Bench was
stronger than an elephant and louder than the
blast of the last trumpet. It heaved hair-
raising, soul-scorching expressions at Killin,
and hurled the whole dictionary of vituperation
at that degraded object; and rained denuncia-
tions upon him ; and then just when it seemed
that he was about to get years of penal servi-
tude, it climbed to the topmost pinnacle of
wrath and came crashing down with a sentence
of seven days’ imprisonment. The reason
assigned for this proceeding was that Killin' ’s
wife and children would have been left in want
had he been shut up for a longer period ; yet if
that offensive wretch had stolen ten shillings he
would have been duly committed for trial, and
would have spent several months in gaol as a
preliminary, and the law would have solemnly
informed him that his wife and children could
not be taken into consideration, and then,
instead of giving way to boiling-hot indignation
and seven days, it would have sent him to gaol
calmly and dispassionately for eighteen months
ov more. It is an essential part of the
inbred brutality of the Anglo-Saxon race
that it cannot even begin to realise the
meaning of cruelty. It is utterly incapable of
comprehending that it is a worse outrage to
maim and batter and disfigure a human being
than to steal 10s.—the fact is as far from it as
a hog is from the stars. And even though the
value of humanity, as compared to that of mere
property, were preached and taught and'ex-
pounded in every possible way with years of
ceaseless iteration, the result would be just the
same—the information is as hopelessly wasted
as if it were fired into the interior of a boiler,
for the magistrate and the boiler are equally
incapable of appreciating it.
* # #
This spirit of unconscious brutality accom-
panies the Briton to all parts of the earth.
England tne other day a “lady” named
Brown was convicted of a series of horrible
assaults on her pauper servant-girl, which ended


in the child’s death—in other words, she was
flogged to death in a slow, lingering way for
BboVn’s amusement, and that “lady” was
sentenced to six months’ hard labour. And, as
usual, the Court informed Brown that she was
a wretch who was unfit to live and not good
enough to die, and apparently it was firmly
convinced that it was wreaking a horrible
revenge on Brown, and coming down on that
alleged female with revolting severity. Yet,
two days later, a hawker who stole a halfpenny
was sentenced to eighteen months’ imprison-
ment, and in this case the Law was not aggra-
vated, as in the previous instance—it was in a
gentle and contemplative mood, and was rather
inclined to be humane than otherwise. Conse-
quently, it takes three murdered servant-girls to
make one halfpenny, even when a British Judge
is raving mad about the servant-girl and mild
and unbiased about the halfpenny; if the
position had been reversed, and the Judge had
bee'n in a wild frenzy over the halfpenny and in
a coldly-critical mood concerning the servant-
girl, it would probably have taken twenty-four
instead of three. The case of another woman
who shoved a red-hot poker into a child’s
mouth came up for consideration when the
British code was in one of its calmer moments,
and the sentence was,“ seven days,” on account
of the ‘ ‘ delicate condition of the prisoner’s
health and being refreshed by this incident,
the Law rose up and awarded five years’ penal
servitude to an individual who stole £2 25., and
nine months to another individual who killed
his wife with a poker. If this latter person
had destroyed a whole harem with the poker
he would probably have been allowed a reduc-
tion on murdering a quantity, but this point
was not sufficiently elucidated.
# * *
The species of insanity which administers jus-
tice in this manner excites little comment, for
in all Britain and North America and Australia
—in all the Anglo-Saxon world, in fact—there
is hardly one Judge or magistrate or other
judicial authority who knows any better. The
trail of the old Saxon brutality is over the
whole population, so that the savagery of
the law passes unobserved. The individual
who is convicted for the third or fourth time of
stealing a few shillings, and whose total
plunderings amount, perhaps, to £2 10s., spends
the best years of his life in confinement, and
the seasons pass, and the years come and go,
and find him still monotonously pounding rocks
or picking oakum, till old age comes upon him
and he is discharged, a useless, broken-down
wreck of no account. And even then his
punishment is only half done, for there is no
rest for him on this side of the grave. If he
tries to find honest employment, for want of
which he was driven to a paltry crime, he is
haunted by the clumsy, ignorant, and stony-
souled detective, who informs every possible
employer of his past misdeeds, and makes
reformation absolutely impossible. If he fails
to find employment—which he necessarily
does—he is arrested for “ vagrancy,” and
receives a fresh sentence of twelve months as a
hardened offender who is “well known to the
police,” and when his term expires the same old
experience begins over, again. If he is seen
anywhere he is arrested and sent to gaol on a
charge of “ being a suspected person frequent-
ing a public place for the purpose of committing
a felony,” and if he is not seen anywhere it is
presumed that he has quitted the district; and
information concerning him is sent to the adja-
cent provinces, so that he may be “moved on ”
if he stops, on the assumption that he has
stopped for a sinful purpose, and stopped if
he moves on under another assumption that
he is moving on for the purpose of
committing some fresh injury to property. And
if he cannot be sufficiently crushed by any or
all of these devices, he can always be reached
on the elastic charge of “having property in
his possession which is reasonably supposed to
be stolen j” and, in short, from the moment of
his first conviction to the last breath of his
miserable existence he is a slave of the most ab-
ject description, and his rights as a human
being are extinguished. It is not even neces-
sary to prove any crime against him ; he can
always be imprisoned on a mere charge of being
a “ suspected person,” and he can be suspected
as often as the most dunderheaded policeman in
existence chooses to suspect him.
* * #
But all this applies to offences against
Property only. The individual, who is
charged with pounding his wretched wife, or
some unoffending stranger, for the eighteenth
time, is fined the same invariable 40s.
or fourteen days, with which he started his
nefarious career, and in his case the cumulative
penalty falls through entirely. He is subjected
to no police surveillance, nor is he systema-
tically ruined and impoverished by the interfer-
ence of the “ force.” He is never arrested on a
charge of ‘ ‘ being reasonably suspected of
having assaulted some person or persons un-
known,” or sent to gaol on a general accusation
°t “being in a public place with intent to
commit a ferocious outrage.” The same blood-
thirsty spirit [(which leads the whole Anglo-
> axon race to put up its money for the pleasure
I see ing two low-grade animals batter and
each other into a muddy and offensive
squash causes it to sympathise with this offen-
ce object. He is a species of informal prize-
ghting exhibition without the gate-money, and
J 16 Pounds or disfigures a woman or a child
** ol ' a full-grown male antagonist, he is
t regarded with a sort of tacit sympathy as
' n artist who is engaged in an inferior branch
lie great science of brutality. The charge
0 sympathising with such an individual would,
0 doubt, be repudiated with boundless indig-
pV? 1 } by every administrator of the law in the
r .. ls h Empire, but the case is one in which
Tff lollB s P ea k m °re plainly than words. The
1 erence between a fine of 40s. and a term of
gi een m'onths’ hard labour sufficiently ex-
{. the feeling of the brutal law 7 -distributor
1 a .. fbe brutal law-breaker is more or less his
oiv i ler ’ as distinguished from the hunger-
Mcken thief who is not his brother in any
t f e whatever. The average Judge or magis-
n , c applies the criminal code—not for the
i ( . 10n °f the nation, but for his own nro-
..i, 1011 ’ an d for that purpose alone. It is
om that he is himself knocked down and
kicked by some brutal rowdy, for he usually
lives in a fashionable suburb where police pro-
tection is abundant, and where the rowdy is
scarce ; and therefore he looks upon the latter
merely as an individual who injures people in
whom he has little interest; but he descends
like an avalanche on the thief for the better
protection of his own hereditary spoons and
forks. And unless a very large number of
Judges and magistrates are violently assaulted
and much damaged it is not likely that the
judicial intelligence will ever awake to a sense
of its own stupendous, time-encrusted idiocy.
# # *
There are one or two exceptions to this great
law of brutality, but they are rather apparent
than real. The garotter is struck with the
biggest sledge-hammer in the legal armoury,
but he is sentenced to a long term of imprison-
ment and a long course of able and vigorous
floggings, not so much because he partially
strangled his victim and then trod on his head,
as by reason of the eighteenpence which he
removed from his pocket after he became insen-
sible. Moreover, the garotter plies his vocation
indiscriminately about the streets, and conse-
quently he is as liable to choke a Judge or a
politician or an archbishop as any other citizen,
and for these reasons the law descends upon
him with all that unobtrusive vigour which it
manifests when either property or the owners
of property are in danger. When this much-
harried offender stays at home and merely
garrottes his own relatives—these being persons
of no political influence, who can be damaged
without striking a blow at any “vested in-
terest ” —the ancient forty-shilling fine fully
meets his case. And in addition to the garrotter
the law falls with tremendous vigour on the in-
dividual who uses a revolver or a knife, even
when he only inflicts a trivial injury in
self-defence, and compared with the individual
who staves in a peaceful stranger or relative
with his fists and his feet, his situation is a bad
one. The reason of this is not very clearly
defined. Most probably, however, fears are
entertained that he might shoot a passing
capitalist by accident, and moreover, his con-
duct is contrai'y to the grand old prize-fighting
instincts of the Anglo-Saxon race. The same
craving which leads the cultured Briton to pay
for the privilege of seeing two men pound each
other till both are practically featureless finds
balm for its soul when somebody is hideously
mashed in a public house row ; but if the defen-
dant cuts the proceedings short, and thereby
defrauds and aggravates the spectators, he
touches the British race on a sensitive point.
The English-speaking nations in all parts of the
world feel towards such an individual much as
the Roman populace would have felt towards,
the person who stunned the tiger with a fence-
rail just as it was about to tear the first mouth-
ful off a promising live deacon, and, therefore,
the criminal code visits him with ponderous
ferocity. Probably the criminal code doesn’t
stop to analyse its own emotions, but that
makes no difference in the result.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The “Ant” Libel Case.
The libel action between an aggravated police-
man and the Melbourne Ant, in Avhich the
defendant newspaper Avas ordered to pay £lOO
damages in order to soothe the feelings of the
injured constable, casts an altogether neAV light
on the position of the press in Australia. The
circumstances out of Avhich the case arose were
somewhat surprising—or rather they would be
surprising if they were not of such exceedingly
frequent occurrence. One day, some months
ago, the constable in question dragged, carried,
humped, or otherAvise propelled a helpless and
insensible individual named Gange to the
police-station, and the customary charge of
“ drunk ” having been entered against him he
was deposited in a cell and left there. After
12 hours, however, it Avas discovered that he
Avasn’t drunk but suffering from fearful injuries
to the head, and about four hours later he died.
Possibly he might have died in any case;
possibly he might have recovered if he had
received immediate attention, mayhap Avas
killed by the long period of neglect to which he
was exposed ; on this point there seems to have
been no reliable evidence. When the medical
examination Avas made 13 hours had elapsed
since Gange’s arrest, and as the doctor’s were
of opinion that his wounds Avere from 12 to 15
hours old it naturally folloAved, either that the
policeman had arrested a dying man in mistake
for a drunk, and that neither he nor any other
official concerned in the case could tell the
difference, or else that he had arrested a com-
mon, ordinary inebriate, and that the injuries
were somehow inflicted while Gangs was in
custody. And here a surprising discrepancy
comes in. The Ant took the former
vieAA 7 , and railed at the officer on a
charge of general stupidity and incapa-
city, yet the officer’s . evidence consisted
chiefly of an impassioned delaration that he had
not battered, assaulted, or ill-used the deceased,
and as the defendants laid no such offence to
his charge this statement —though it must
arouse sympathy—seems to have absolutely no
bearing on the case. This point being disposed
of, there remained only the original accusation
that the officer arrested a badly-injured man in
mistake for an inebriate, and as Judge
in his summing up, declared that the plaintiff
had acted “in a most proper manner” the
damages appear to have been given on the
ground that such a blunder was rather com-
mendable than otherwise. No evidence was
produced to show that anybody except the
constable was to blame in the matter, and as
the court decided that the constable Avas not
to blame it naturally follows that nobody Avas
responsible. It also folloAvs that the death of a
common human being under such circumstances
is not a subject Avhich a neAvspaper can comment
upon Aviih impunity, or at all events it must not
use strong language about it, but should look at
in a quiet, indifferent, unimpassioned manner as
if it was a drainage item or a question of
paving. And the evident moral is, that a news-
paper should not give Avay to excitement about
anything that doesn’t pay, and that the libt?
law is badly in want of closer definition. For
apparently the only thing that the defendants
could have lawfully asserted in such a case was
that Gange was dead, and as everybody knew
that fact it wasn’t worth mentioning.
A “ British Seaman."
At Hobart (Tas.) the other day, half-a-dozen
seamen belonging to a barque then lying in the
harbour struck work and went ashore, contrary
to the orders of the monarch on the quarter-
deck, and, being promptly arrested, they were
sentenced to four weeks’ imprisonment apiece.
There was nothing surprising or unusual about
this proceeding ; such events in the free land of
Australia arc more common than mud or cock-
roaches or unwholesome liquor, and it is a mere
matter of course that the seaman should be
regarded by the law as something about half-
way between a horse and a convicted felon.
Yet there is absolutely no more reason that the
mariner who knocks off work while in harbour
should be sent to gaol than that Parkes should
get 28 days’ hard labour for not turning up at
his office in the morning, or that any other
politician should be seized by a policeman, and
dragged through the gutters, and knocked up
against house corners, and bumped against
lamp-posts, and finally landed in a bruised and
dishevelled condition at the lock-up because he
failed to be in his place when the House met.
In fact, there is much more reason for arresting
the politician than the seaman, for the latter
draws his pay only when he is working, while
the former is paid whether he works or not, and
even a Chinese intellect can appreciate the
glaring injustice of a system by which one man
has his pay stopped and is sent to gaol
as well, while the other man commits
precisely the same offence without any
results at all. The seaman, in fact, is
one of the anomalies of civilisation. He is a
miserably-paid individual, who lives in a heav-
ing dog-kennel, and works long hours in fine
weather, while in bad weather he works inces-
santly, and subsists on horrible victuals, and
leads an existence as precarious as that of a
man who lives by falling over precipices, and
while at sea he is practically the slave and hard-
driven nigger of an autocrat who is beyond law
and out of the reach of justice. The last con-
dition is supposed to be necessary for the safety
of his vessel, but this theory falls through, for
even in harbour he is sent to gaol if he “ refuses
to obey orders,” or if he quits his vessel leaving
his wages behind him; and if he goes on shore
to lodge a complaint against a brutal officer, fie
is generally run in for being absent without
leave ; and yet the nation which regards these
things as a matter of course would rend the
heavens with its shriek of indignation if the
P.M. who tries the case were kicked into a
mouldy dungeon when he is “ absent without
leave,” or if some Civil Service official were
hauled away by the collar and the hair because
he left the office at lunch-time and neglected to
come back. The seaman, however, seldom has
a vote, and the foreign seaman, w’ho drops in
casually at an Australian port, and is punctually
gaoled for trying to get ashore to look for
justice, never has one ; and in this fact
lies the secret of his misfortunes. No poli-
tician cares one solitary cent about a man
who has no vote and no political weight. No
judge or magistrate cares a cent about him
either, for it is a dead certainty that no one
will take up his case in Parliament, and there-
fore any bald and unsupported assertion of an
officer is sufficient, in most cases, to outweigh
the evidence of an entire crew. No lawyer
cares about him, for he has seldom any money
to pay a lawyer, and an unpaid lawyer doesn’t
care even about an archangel. No consul cares
about him, for sailors are the principal bane and
trouble of a consul’s existence —in short, nobody
cares except a disinterested Christian, and there
fs only one disinterested Christian to every
thousand square leagues of the earth’s surface,
and when that rare individual is found he
has no money and no influence, and can’t do
anything, and labours under the suspicion of
being a lunatic. And therefore the seamen’s
position, taking it all round, is not a thing that
even a dog could afford to envy.

1“ Putting 1 Down ” Homicide.
The Victorian assassination boom still keeps
moving, and the Judges keep on passing
sentences and preaching solemn homilies, and
the hangman keeps on hanging, all just as
before. There is a woeful sameness about the
sentences and the hangings, but the solemn
preachments of the Bench are, perhaps, about
the most monotonous of all. The following is
a slab from the report of a recent trial at Sale,
on which occasion the Court delivered itself of
these memorable words :
In these days the colonies appear to be passing
through an epidemic of murders and manslaughters, so
that to-day human life is less regarded than at any other
time within my memory. It is absolutely essential that
this state of things should be put down.
It is only the same old flail pounding the same
old straw that has been pounded through all the
ages. Every man who is furnished with under-
standing knows that an effect cannot be ‘ ‘ put
down” unless the cause is also extinguished,
and as yet nobody really know 7 s and hardly any-
body cares to ask what is the cause of homi-
cide. It is not, as a rule, a matter of profit; in
most cases it pays not a single cent in the way
of dividend, and is one glaring, conspicuous loss
from first to last. In this respect it resembles
w 7 ar, which is ruin to the nation which wins
and still greater ruin to the nation that does not
win, and so long as war exists it is quite
evident that man will play at a game where
there is nothing to be gained and everything to
be lost. If murder can be “ put down,” then
war can be put down likewise, for in
each case it is only necessary to convince
anybody of the insanity of being killed
for no visible purpose. To extinguish war,
however, is much the easier undertaking of the
two, for all that is necessary to produce a
murder is that the nation should grow one
solitary individual who is sufficiently wanting
in intelligence to make an exhibition of himself
at the end of.a greased rope when there is no
profit in the transaction, while a war conducted
on modern principles requires 50,000 such
lunatics at the very least. It would take
28,000 hangings to do as much in the way of
eradicating this species of derangement as was
done on the single battlefield of Gravelotte, and
as Gravelotte did nothing whatever to lessen
the unaccountable tendency of mankind to go
out and be killed gratis, it is obvious that even
28,000 hangings are a very small and ineffectual
thing indeed. Under such conditions as these
a Judge only makes himself an object of public
derision when he puts on frills over one hanging
as a means of putting down anything, for ex-
perience has long since proved either that hang-
ing is no more the way to put down murder
than gunshot is to put down war, or else that it
wants more hanging or more gunshot than the
world has ever yet seen to make the slightest
impression on either disease. Moreover, the
man who undertakes to eradicate homicide by
execution is obviously beginning at the wrong
end. The first step should betoput down common
assault, for homicide in most cases is merely an
assault in which one party has hit rather harder
than usual. And common assault belongs to
the same class of grievances as Parliamentary
opposition, religious controversy, prize-fighting,
boxing, scientific disputes, riot, insurrection,
and argument generally, for they are all signs
and evidences of a combative instinct, which is
in itself unaccountable and which cannot be ex-
tinguished simply because of its unaccountable-
ness. Also strikes, -party processions, shooting,
clerical reform, horse-racing, patriotism, and
dog-fights, are all more or less dependent on
the same principle, and unless they can all be
wiped out homicide will still remain in exist-
ence. Therefore, the Judge who resolves to ‘ ‘ put
down ” manslaughter has undertaken the largest,
widest, and most comprehensive contract on
record, and has cheerfully entered on a job at
which a million archangels might break their
backs without making the first beginnings of an
impression. And, strangely enough, there was
no shriek of uncontrollable laughter when he
made his great announcement. The audience
heard this undersized fly declare his intention
of removing a ton of bricks at one lift and never
moved a muscle.
Baron Hirsch, who denies that he offered
£3,000,000 for the relief of the persecuted
Russian Jews, has apparently stopped Tummy’s
supplies, and that worthy, fearing to ask Par-
liament for pecuniary help, has fallen back on
“Ma,” who, out of her store (said to be 13
millions), intends to wipe off his liabilities. A
Sydney paper puts down Walks’ income at
£50,000, including the allowance of £lO,OOO to
his wife, but that is a long way under the mark.
For instance, he receives £16,210 15s. annually
as a perpetual pension from the Duchy of Corn-
wall for ‘ ! the loss of duty on the coinage of tin and
in lieu of ” —we smile—“ post groats and white
rents.” Then, again, he lias another small per-
petual pension from Cornwall for the loss of
“ prisage and butlerage on wine” imported
through that country. Furthermore, the average
net returns to his coffers per year from the
Duchy since 1864 have been £61,232. In addi-
tion to these enormous sums he draws salaries as
Field Marshal, as Colonel of the Tenth Hussars
(£1350), and sums from various other military
positions wdiich he holds. His travelling ex-
penses, when his movements can by any means
be put down as State ones, are charged to the
country, as are the repairs of Marlborough
House, wdiich average £5OOO yearly. It may
be forgotten that on Wales coming of age and
getting married he received a grant of £23,455,
and from the Duchy of Cornwall the accumula-
tions of his minority, £572,075 in the Funds and
£29,646 in the Bank. Poor pauper !

Ex-Parson Cullen, who represents North
Sydney in the N. S. W. Assembly, was last week
fatuous enough to publicly disparage Grey in
favour of Parkes. ‘‘ If they only knew the
two men they would find Sir Henry Parkes a
truer Democrat than Sir George Grey.” Now,
Cullen doesn’t know Grey —he only knows his
lord and master and godfather, Parkes.
Cullen says that Grey “ lacks sagacity.” He
should have said that Grey lacks hypocrisy.
“Parkes is in reality a truer friend of One-
Man-One-Vote than Grey, for he has embodied
it in a Bill which they are about to pass.” Now-,
Grey has embodied it in a Bill which has
passed, notwithstanding that he submitted it
merely as a private member. “As a leader,
Parkes is immeasurably superior to Grey.”
As a party leader, perhaps. Grey was born,
however, not to lead a party, but to lead a nation.
From the manner in which Cullen attacked
Grey and glorified Parkes it can easily be seen
that the latter is cut to the quick by Grey’s
magnificent and unexampled reception in Aus-
tralia, where he is now the god of the Demo-
cracy. His action in agitating for One-Man-
One-Vote as the bedrock of the Federal Consti-
tution, and standing almost alone in so doing,
has secured for him that picturesque place in
Australian history which the shifty Parkes
would fain have occupied.

Jock Nimmo is Held to have been ignorant of
the fact that he applied for 500 new 7 shares in
the Premier Building Association, instead of 100
shares. And although he pocketed interest on
500 half-crown deposits, and not on 100, a
learned Judge has decided that Nimmo was too
utterly devoid of business capacity to know
what he w r as doing, or why he did it. Being so
uncommonly soft-headed, perhaps Nimmo
doesn’t yet understand that he still draws £6
per week as a member of Parliament, and used
for some years to draw a plump salary for
“managing” a ministerial department. Who
was it that said—“ Go forth, my son, and see
with how little wisdom the world is governed ” ?
By the w'ay, they say that even Nimmo requires
a change of scene, and when he clears out there
will be only Dow left to meet that £175,000 for
which the P.B.A. directors are alleged to be
jointly and severally liable “under the Act.”
There’s no getting away from the Act except by
mail steamer, apparently. Lumsden slunk
away before the trials began ; Gourlav and his
friend, Murphy, are enjoying themselves in
Europe ; Ferguson and .Stewart were not to
be found the last time they w'ere enquired for ;
and Jock Nimmo will have to travel for the
benefit of his health. Not that it can make
much pecuniary difference to the poor old man,
whether he goes or stays. It was the other
fellows who got hold of the half millions at a
time.


In Queensland now, if two men meet and fight
anywhere or under any circumstances what-
ever, it transpires afterwards in the daily
papers that the man who was knocked over
was a “free labourer,” while the man who
knocked him over was a unionist. This kind of
assault arid battery is getting so monotonous that
wo are moved to inquire why somebody
doesn’t infuse some variety into the matter. Is
wery blackleg so thin and helpless and gone at
the knees that he falls down when he and a
unionist start hitting each other? or do the
papers simply call the man who is on top a
unionist, from sheer force of habit; or are
the assaults mostly bogus? As matters
stand at present it would seem that, however
big and brawny the blackleg may be, and how-
ever small and old the unionist, the former
simply sits down on the pavement and weeps
when he sees the latter coming, and allows
himself to be walloped without resistance, and
naturally this circumstance looks improbable
as well as monotonous. Moreover, the non-
union shearer appears to turn up in the most
unlikely places, and everybody who is assaulted
or injured up North seems to be he. About a
week ago, for instance, a rowdy crowd looted
a pub. on the Warrego, and according to report
they emptied out the liquor and tied up the
landlord’s family to, a log by the wayside.
There ' was something refreshing about this
story despite sundry improbabilities in the
narrative, for the landlord was the only outside
party who seemed to. have been battered or
insulted in the North for some months ; and
therefore it was a woeful disappointment to
find at the tail-end of the telegram that even
he was a “ non-union shearer,” though why a
publican should go shearing while lambing-
down i§ so much easier and more profitable is, a
mystery. This sort of thing is wearisome. Is
there not one non-unionist in, all Queensland,
who, is large enough to hit somebody back ?
.
The latest rumour in British financial circles is
that all the. big finance houses in London, Paris,
Berlin, and Vienna, with the single exception
of Rothschilds, are in, a desperately tight
place over the foreign-loan industry, and if one
of them goes there will probably be an awful
smash and about 40 millionaires will vanish
bpneath the debris. It was so easy in the old
days for a banker to lend his own money to
some busted empire at the rate of £5O or £6O
for every £lOO debenture, and then softly un-
load on the idiotic public at £9O, that the
financiers hardly realised the situation when
the public stopped buying and left the last
batch of loans on their hands. About once
every year, these speculators used to lend every-
thing, down to their clothes and boots, to any
insolvent State that wanted money, and after-
wards pass the loss on to the nation generally
at an advanced figure. The process only
needed to miss fire once, and the syndicates
were left on the instant with tons of paper
securities and hardly enough ready cash to buy
a sandwich. When a bank buys up a
£20,000,009 loan and can’t see it again, it is
obvious that that bank is in a tight place, and
when it has to find another loan next year to
keep the borrowing Government from going
bankrupt it is still worse, for it can’t raise the
new loan through all its capital being sunk in
the old one, and if it doesn’t do it the old loan
isn’t worth anything, and all the capital dis-
appears there and then. This sort of thing is
unpleasant for the banker, but in the end it does
a lot of good to the world at large. When a
man has got about a hundred-thousand times
his fair share of the world’s wealth and tries to
keep on doubling his pile at short intervals, it
is obvious that he or his descendants must own
the earth some day and leave nothing for any-
body else unless a smash comes.
The same old giddy infant rushing miserably
into destruction has turned up once more—this
time at Dunedin (Maoriland). She was a small
female child of eleven, -with an old, old eye and
a look of ancient wisdom on her crinkled
features, and she called round in a calm, self-
possessed way at the police-station, and told
the inspector how she had been “ assaulted ” by
Andrew Manson, artizan. She was such a
parched, dried-up prehistoric-looking infant
that the officer was staggered, and though he
hardly believed her, she swore affidavits and
took oaths with such surprising fluency that
there was nothing for it but to run Manson in.
The accused appeared at the police-court next
morning, and tried in a dazed, astonished way
to find out what it was all about; but his
accuser climbed into the witness-box with a
considerable exhibition of white stockings and
undergarments and swarmed up on a chair so
that her head might appear over the top, and
talked the court nearly to death in about two
minutes. Then she danced down again, and
the bench committed Manson for trial, and
went home wondering at the prematurely aged
and blase aspect of the prosecutor. And finally
the Assizes came on, but by this time Manson
had recovered his senses sufficiently to hire a
lawyer, and that person drew the infant out till
she abandoned all considerations of time and
space, and made statements that were contrary
to the laws of gravitation and cohesion and
everything else; and then she burst into tears
and acknowledged that she had got the whole
story from another girl, and that the other girl
had got it out of a newspaper. Whereupon
she climbed out of the witness-box with
another exhibition of white stockings and
undergarments, and fled for home. The story
doesn’t go any further. If this infant with the
ancient eye and the old understanding had per-
jured herself over a case of petty larceny there
would have been trouble, but no one ever
dreams of prosecuting the girl who has faked up
an indecent story with a view to getting a man
—often a complete stranger—sent into penal
servitude. There are about three dozen such
girls loose in various parts of Australasia, and
they are mostly prospering.
A young man, from beyond the Alps,
turned up in Rome with such a perfect likeness to
the Emperor Augustus that the Emperor sent for
him. Emperor : “ Young man, was your mother
over in Rome?” The Stranger ; “No, sire ; but
tny father was.”

It is some years since “ Masks and Faces”
(by no means a very striking comedy) was seen in
Sydney, and, so far as we know, it has never pre-
viously been so well staged and played as now at
the Garrick. This sentence is penned, however,
more in condemnation of the past than in com-
mendation of the_ present, for, owing chiefly to the
weakness of Triplet and Pomander, the show
never gets, from start to finish, a good grip on the
audience. J anet Aohurch’s Peg is a very credit-
able and intelligent impersonation, but it lacks
vivacity and is more likely to please those who
see the lady for the first time than those
to whom her mannerisms items, uncertain
contralto voice, peaked - out chin, dilated
eyes, aiul colourless utterance of occasional
sentences—are familiar. Miss Achurch, however,
is at all times very much in earnest and
that is sufficient palliation for a multitude of
peculiarities. Chas. Charrington is a capable
actor, inside his limited range, and an intelligent
man, but nature never intended him to play
Triplet. He has no muscular adaptability, either
of voice or feature; his sobs are suspiciously like
guffaws ; his simulation of grief wouldn’t deceive
a sucking-child ; he habitually mispronounces his
vowels and invariably emphasises his consonants,
and, as Triplet, is a, well-meaning but melancholic
and monotonous mummer. In fact, the oast is
cursed with monotony of intonation. Eille
Norwood’s Pomander gabbles his lines with
an up-and-down inflection that suggests the
pitching of a row-boat in a tideway ; Emma
Bronton’s speeches flow forth with the smooth-
ness of soothing syrup, and Harry Power’s
Quin is simply a boor who has hardly a redeeming
feature. On the other hand, Herbert Flemming’s
V ane is finished and altogether lovely; George
Leitch’s Colley Cibber is a very clever character
sketch ; the Soaper and Snarl of Owen Harris and
William Holman are excel-
lently made-up, harmonious-
ly handled. In very minor
parts, Trent Adams’ Burdock
has a good dialect, and there
are no holes, in Charles
Westmacott’s Colander. We
nearly forgot to make men-
tion of the Kitty Clive of
Alice May, the lady’s first
big show. It is brightly
vivacious and easy, and
otherwise lacks nothing but
the height and vocal force
that nature has withheld.
The fair Janet got a
tremendous reception on
first night from, perhaps, the
biggest audience the Garrick
has yet admitted. Mr.
Wilson’s scenery deserves a
line in its praise, but some
of the furniture at Vane’s
house is surely not of “ the
period.”
sc- * sc *
“.The Bells of Hasle-
mere,” by Petti tt and
Grundy, like all the other
pot-boiling melodramas of
the period, is a mechanical
play, a mere medium for
sensational scene-shifting
and dramatic tableaux.
Perhaps its language is a
little less stilted and tauto-
logical than tli at of its
immediate predecessors on
Sydney Royal boards, but
its characters are even more
stereotyped and infinitely
less human. Some of its
villains are mere cardboard
silhouettes, who are villainous
without any apparent
motive except the will of
the author. Indeed, Thorndyke (W. H. Seagrave)
is the most unsatisfactory and inexplicable
scoundrel that was ever guyed by a gallery.
Silkstone (Albert Norman), the chief robber,
belongs to that variety of villain who sets his
heart on some other fellow’s girl or wife.
He doesn’t go the right way to win her ; he puts
the bailiff in her parents’ humble home, or that of
her friends, and occasionally drags her round by
the hair in order to arouse her dormant affection,
nevertheless is surprised to the staggering point
at each rebuff and never fails to take the gallery
into his confidence when he says : “Beware, proud
girl, the day may come when you will be compelled
to love me !” This sort of thing always reminds
us of an episode witnessed one night in Bourke-
street. AGeorgiaminstrel was fishinghimself outof
one of the odorous canals peculiar to Melbourneand
Venice. His bell-topper and gold-headed cane
were just disappearing under one of the numerous
bridges that crossed the stream, but, ere going in
pursuit, he shook his fist at the retreating form of
the fastidious nymph who had treated him to a
gratuitous wash and gurgled out with spouts of
gutter-water: “You daisy; I’ll hev you yit! ”
This piece has one peculiarity, it affords its
actresses little opportunity. Nevertheless, Norah
(the most human and understandable character in
the play), handled by Maggie Knight (she
hasn’t been seen in Sydney since she fell out of
Jennie Lee’s revival of “The Ticket-of-Leave
Man ’’ through illness), with a delicious brogue, is
the brightest and most interesting impersonation
in the play. Yore, the adventurer, ably repre-
sented by W. E. Baker (in striking contrast to his
Chicago Irishman) runs Norah a good second, and
is one of his best efforts up to date. Roberts’
Capt. Salem is fairly good, but no one else is more
than supportable, and Bland Holt’s Blacksmith
Armstrong is his worst on record, and even his
best friends in the gallery seemed delighted on
first night when lie scorched nis fingers in the
horse-shoe making scene. The big feature of the
show is Gordon’s scenery, and the manner in
which the numerous changes are made on an open
stage in full view of the audience. “ The Bells ”
are hung up for three weeks; it lias not yet been
decided what new piece they will ring in.
** * *
Contrary to first intentions, “The Silver
Shield,” by Grundy, succeeded “Imprudence,”
but is revived for six nights only. In charming
contrast to some of the sloppy work he has done
in melodrama, this is a cleverly-written, smartly-
conceived comedy, whoso failure to catch on
previously may have been due to the fact that
much of its humour is couched in stage-slang, and
is therefore not understanded of the multitude. It
seems to go better now than on the former
occasion, however, though not apparently better
impersonated. Cecil Ward has succeeded Percy
Lyndal (what lovely romantic names some actors
have!) as Tom Potter; Arthur Lawrence follows
Hilton as Sir Humphrey Chetwynd ; Geo. L.
Gordon, as Dr. Dozey, steps into the slippers and
night-cap of Geo. Leopold ; Frank Cates imper-
sonates Ned, formerly in the hands of “Bob”
Inman, and Lucy Preston, formerly played by
Miss Gilmore, is now assumed by Brenda Gibson.
“ Turned-Up,” preceded by “ Barbara,” will pro-
bably be put up on Saturday.
“The Corsican Brothers” will fade away
into the ewigkeit from Her Majesty’s stage on
Saturday next. All the machinery and the print-
ing for “The County Fair,” forwarded from
America, via Britain, to save trans-continental
freight, has not yet come to hand, so “Siberia,”
by Bartley Campbell, played at this house soon
after its opening, will probably go up in its stead
for a brief space. As this drama deals with the
persecution of Russian Jews, it will have peculiar
interest at present.

The skating season has been started at
Coogee With several loud bangs caused by the
impact of Manager Larmonr’s fattest friends and
the flooring-boards. A large hotel to contain fifty
bedrooms is shortly to be erected in the paddock
at a cost of £12,000. It will be conducted on
continental principles and floated into a company,
with a guarantee of six per cent, for four years on
subscribed capital.

The Majeroni benefit was, perhaps, the
most successful theatrical benefit that ever took
place in Australia. Her Majesty’s was crowded,
everywhere, even in the gallery, and the whole
areas of dress-circle and stalls were reserved long
prior to the rising of the curtain. Actors,
managers, theatre-hands, printers, bill-posters,
ushers, musicians, and representative theatre.
MISS MAGGIE KNIGHT.
goers, fairly tumbled over, one another in their
efforts to be of service, and one gentleman, Phil,
Glenister, the well-known metallician, actually
vvas responsible for the sale of 250 five-shilling
tickets. When such enthusiasm was displayed,
it is not surprising that tne sum realised should
have topped by £5O that (£400) obtained by the
benefit in this house for the Bourke Relief Fund,
albeit that was for a public calamity. Illness
prevented Signor Majeroni from attending, but a
tender and mournful letter of thanks and farewell
from this great artiste, generous and whole-souled
man, was read by George Rignold to a deeply-
interested audience.
* * *
Maggie Knight is a native of Maoriland
and went on the stage, as soon as she could lisp,
in Auckland. Since then she has travelled all
over Australasia and made one trip round the
world, playing in everything from burlesque to
tragedy. She impersonated Rosalind, Portia,
Ophelia, &0., at Melbourne Royal, and, with
Wybert Reeve, in Adelaide, we imagine, was the
first Kate Verity in “The Squire,” and Dora in,
“ Diplomacy.”
** * *
“The Middleman” has only been a
partial success at the Adelaide Royal, for Charles
Cartwright’s acting is considered extravagant,
and it has received some unpleasant criticism.
The Batty Todd of Tom Bolton is locally looked
on as the most ludicrous piece of acting seen for
many a day. One moment he may be considered
a Scotchman, at another a Jew, then an attempt
is made at the Yorkshire dialect. Olga Nether-
sole is excellent as usual, Edward Sass, Mary
Kingsley, and others render valuable support!;
but why import Cosgrove to play Captain Chand-
ler, and leave Laurence Cautley out of the cast?
“The Idler,” by special request, will be revived
ere the season closes. The Darrells will then pro-
duce “The English Rose,” supported by Lau-
rence Cautley and Mary Kingsley.

It is better to be born under a plucky

The phylloxera invades the champagne
country, but there are 40,000,000 bottles in stock
at Rheims, enough for 10 years.
While Messrs. Mitchell, of Brunambrong,
Upper Murray, were at the marriage of their
sister in Melbourne, some miscreant unknown
drove seven of their blood mares into a gully and
shot them with a rifle.
Johnston, the Ballarat man who slew his
wife and family, has been temporarily reprieved
at the instance of two well-known Ballarat
medical men who, on the night before the ap-
pointed day of execution, certified to his insanity.
How they managed to gain admission to the gaol
and the prisoner under the circumstances has not
yet transpired. On the other hand Wilson, the
obscure convict who was ordered to die for
criminal assault on a child, and who probably is
quite as mad as Johnston, duly died by the hand
of the executioner at the appointed time. A news-
paper-report states that on the scaffold Wilson
“looked round to see whether Johnston was
there.” No, dear friends, Johnston was not there.
The “ well-connected ” man is never there under
such circumstances. What a lovely satire ’twould
have been had Wilson remarked out loud to the
Sheriff ; “Where is my well-connected friend, Mr.
Johnston this morning ?”
The N.S. W. Department of Public Instruc-
tion has issued toits teachers a small volume of 150
pages, entitled, “Drink, and Strong Drink. ” From
a notice pasted inside we learn, first, that the
book is the property of the Department; next,
that the teacher is to mark, read and digest the
contents, but not to commit himself to any
extreme views when teaching—which is pretty
calm, considering that Dr. Benjamin Ward
Richardson, the gifted author, is nothing when
not a teetotal fanatic and that his book is from
beginning to end nothing but a never-touch-it
sermon. The Bulletin does not object to this,
for alcohol so eloquently sings its own praises that
it is just as well for every boy and girl to start out
in life with a prejudice against it. However, the
volume should be withdrawn from circulation at
once, as it embodies a deliberate lie which comes
in thus : at the end of the book that pure and
altogether lovely old man, Longfellow, who liked
wine, is dragged in as a total-abstinence poet,
three verses from a poem of his (the name of which
“Catawba Wine ” is artfully suppressed) being
quoted in praise of water and in denunciation of
alcoholic drink :
Now, of course, the inference sought to be drawn
by this bald quotation is that “ wine is a mocker,”
&c,, bub the truth is that Longfellow, when he wrote
the verses, was chanting, not the praises of water,
but of the wine of California, a province of his
native land, as witness the verse that immediately
follows the above in Longfellow’s poem, but which
is not quoted in the State-school book :
While pure as a spring
Is the wine I sing,
And to praise it one needs but name it ;
For Catawba wine
Has need of no sign,
No tavern bush to proclaim it..
And here are preceding verses, also carefully sup-
pressed
This song of mine
Is a song of the vine,
To be sung by the glowing embers
Of the way-side inns
When the rain begins
To darken the drear Novembers.
For richest and best
Is the wine of the West
That grows by the Beautiful River,
Whose sweet perfume
Fills all the room
With a benison on the giver.
Very good in its way
Is the Verzenay,
Or the Sillery soft and creamy;;
But Catawba wine
Has taste more divine,
More dulcet, delicious and dreamy. _
After all, considering that Australia is predestined
(“foredoomed,” the water-cranks might call U
to become one of the greatest of the world s
producing countries, it is doubtful whether, m
long run, the State would not do more good y
teaching its children to drink one part of non
native red wine with two parts of water at 111 ,
time than to cram them with teetotal poe >
under false pretences. Just read over the t
verses from Longfellow (apart from the con ,
as they are printed in the State school-book, .
see what a flavour of Moody-and-Sankey^
snuffle is imported into the whole affair by
mention of the Beautiful River—as thoug
referred to “the Jordan,” or a river runni
Heaven, or some other article of the pa
stock-in-trade !
There grows no wine
By the haunted Rhine,
By the Danube or Guadalquiver,
Nor on island or cape,
That hears such a grape
As grows by the Beautiful River.
Drugged is their juice
For foreign use,
When shipped o’er the reeling Atlantic
To rack our brains
With fever pains
That have driven the Old World frantic.
To the sewers and sinks
With all such drinks,
And after them tumble the mixer,,
For a poison malign
Is such Borgia wine,
Or at best, but a “ Devil’s Elixir.!’

Melbourne Standard, evidently desiring to bestow
butter, referred to “that good, old-fashioned Adelphi
drama, ‘The Courier of Lyons.”’ After this, Dampier
may well pray to be saved from his newspaper friends.
“ The Courier of Lyons” is a work of art founded on his-
torical fact, and no more resembles the typical Adelphi
rubbish (of which “ Lights o’ London ” is'a modern imita-
tion! than ’Enry Parkes resembles George Washington. It
takes rank amongst the stage classics in France and Eng-
land, and will, no doubt, be supplying material for
plagiarists until the crack of doom.

Some obscure periodical has wounded the feelings of
Lance Lenton by announcing that he sings his own songs
at the Gaiety Hall, Melbourne, “with great success,
whereas he merely pleads guilty to writing some of them.
Lenton has, in fact, left the modern stage to its fate, and
swears that he will never return, except to play Macbeth
for a short season of farewell benefits.
Barry Sullivan—no matter what his Australian admirers
may say—never had anything of a reputation in London,
and his last engagement at Brury Lane, in 187 C, was a
black frost. Irving had been playing “Richard the
Third” at the Lyceum only a few’ months earlier, and
Whilst lie, with all his angularities and mannerisms,
looked like a Scholar born to command, Barry Sullivan
represented the old bellowing butcher-breed of Richard—
a penny plain and twopence coloured Crookback. The
most trustworthy record of Richard credits him with a
handsome face, polished manner, and cultured mind—a
great man, who had the Bevii mostly perched on his
deformed shoulder. Miln’s will be a fine performance of
Richard when he can be persuaded to shake off the Bevii
occasionally, and to act it throughout instead of reserving
himself for the big speeches.

close my eyes for a second, and I hear a thunderous out-
burst. I start, sit bolt upright and see Sara, who has
entered the church and joined Mario. She stands and
bows, displacing Sardou’s verbal calisthenics for the sake
ot responding to the welcome of a ‘ brilliant ’ audience,
then drops instantly from Sara Bernhardt to Floria Tosca

the play proceeds.”

A new American theatre has been named “ The Colum-
bus.” That’s one big point in favour of Americans, they
don’t slavishly copy from older countries. We have
“ Royals,” “ Princesses,” Gaietys,”and “ Operas ” all over
Australia, and not one name for a theatre that has not
come down to us from our ancestors on a play-bill.
While Charles Mathews occupied London Lyceum, he
was playing a season to poor houses which one evening
he augmented by a liberal output of “paper.” That
night the gallery was very noisy and not a word could be
heard at times. At length Charles marched down to the
footlights and said: “ Gentlemen of the gallery, although
you have paid for your seats, you have no right to disturb
the comfort of the people in the stalls who have not.’

When the late Barry Sullivan was running a double
company at the old Melbourne Royal, and producing
first-class pieces, Charles Kean, under the guidance of
George Coppin and the patronage of Governor Sir
Charles Darling, was playing at the old Haymarket.
Darling was almost every night at Kean’s show, owing, it
was said, to a special tip from “ home.” Sullivan wrote
asking the Governor’s patronage on a particular evening,
and received the A.D.O.’s reply that “ Sir Charles could
not patronise any other theatre than the Haymarket
while Mr. Kean was playing there.” He enclosed, how-
ever, a cheque for the vice-regal box for the night. This
Sullivan promptly returned with the curt information
that he wanted the Governor's patronage and not his
money. A Governor’s money is worth more than his
patronage even to-day.
That is a smart retort in “Imprudence” when the

“Do you wish to break my heart?” The reply, sobbed
out: “Stone-breaking isn’t woman’s work,” fetches them.

Frank Burgh and Emma Burgh, man and wife, ar e
simply making a fortune, by exhibiting themselves all
over Germany; they have already done Vienna and
Berlin. They are youngish people, and the attraction is
the amazing tattoo allover their bodies, of which they
show as much as possible, without shook, for tattoo has a
curious effect, as of clothing, and quite takes away the
idea of nakedness. Frank has the Crucifixion beauti-
fully tattooed on his chest, and Emma has the Last Supper
done with equal skill upon her back. One of her legs is
tattood all over with emblems of Ireland, and the other
with emblems of America. Frank has . a fine portrait of
Emma on his stomach. It would almost take a to
describe all the picture-tattooing. For the time being
this show runs away with the money. The Burghs are
Irish-^mericana.

Says the London correspondent of the Adelaide AdvER
I’iSKR t —“ Three years ago when Madame Melba came
home resolved to become a great priina donna she
separated amicably from her husband, Mr. Armstrong.
Matrimony must not, they resolved, burke her artistic
career. Now, however, that the goal has been reached,
and the fair Australian stands second only as a songstress
to Patti, there seems no further necessity to repress the
softer emotions, so when Mr. Armstrong arrived home
three weeks ago he was received practically as well as
metaphorically with open arms. The couple have re-
solved to live together henceforward, Mr. Armstrong
giving up his business in Australia to look after his bril-
liant wife’s affairs in Europe. He will take a run out to
sell up his colonial properties, and then settle permanently
in Europe. Melba is, of course, in the way of becoming a
rich woman.”
Colonel Henry Maplesoh, the once mighty entrepre-
neur, lately separated from Marie Roze, has married
Laura Schirmer Byron, the beautiful thirty-year-old
American priind donna, who, of her own accord, entered,
it is said, the Sultan’s seraglio at Constantinople,- but
somehow (assuming that she ever was in the harem)
escaped, married Byron, the British tenor, and opened an
opera-house at Constantinople. It failed, and Byron
drowned himself in a Hellespont of booze. Lord Lytton
assisted at the ceremony. He naturally would.
Nudity having reached its zenith in the stark bareness
of Sybil Johnstone as Ira in “The Cleraenceau Case,” a
reaction has set in against the wearing of tights through-
out America, and the majority of the leading burlesque
ladies have flatly refused to don anything but skirts in
future. It is to be hoped they’ll stick to the resolution,
for the ordinary woman, inkneed, big stomached, and
loose bosomed, is a hideously uninviting object when not
“fixed up.” The sculptors and painters are responsible
for the false ideas of feminine loveliness that permeate the
masculine brain. Your Venus of Milo, Power's Greek
Slave, the Aphrodite of Titians, the Chloes of half-a-dozen
painters, and the Dutch-built beauties of Rubens, are
symbols of feminine beauty—the perfections of a dozen
imperfect women skillfully pieced together—and by no
means truthful representations of feminine shapeliness.
In fact, select at random half-a-dozen men from any class,
and they will be found in point of shapeliness, individual
for individual, immeasurably superior to any similarly
selected six women of similar age and condition. As an
aesthetic spectacle, full-grown man is miles ahead of full-
grown woman, whose loveliness in 99 cases out of 100 is
a mere figment of the imagination.
Sarah Bernhardt’s proper (Christian) name is Rosine
She was born at Paris, Oct. 22, 1844, and is a Jewess of
French and Dutch parentage. Her early life was chiefly
spent at the house of her grandfather, an Amsterdam
optician. Entered Paris Conservatoire 1858, second prize
for tragedy 1881, and for comedy 1862. First stage
appearance, Theatre Francais, as Racine’s Iphigiinie and ,
Scribe’s Valerie. Failure. Acted burlesque at Gymnase
and Porte St. Martin. At the Odeon, 1867, made a mark,
culminating with Marie di Neuborg, in “ Ruy Bias.”
Returned to the Francais, and was thenceforward mighty.
The apex, zenith, and apogee of Christmas pantomimes
in London was reached 1862, when there were 21, all clever,
original, successful; Byron furnishing 4 splendid ones, the
Covent Garden, Adelphi, Strand, and St. James’s ! Nel-
son wrote 300 pantomimes, and Akhurst, of Melbourne,
fully held his own with the Blanchards, Broughs, Tal-
fourds, and Burnands. Why, they turned out Bvangehnes
by the bushel.
Julia Edouin is dead. Tire Edouin family, as before the
English and Australian public 35 years ago, consisted of
Charles, John, Eliza, Rose, Willie, and Julia, the last four
child-actors. Charles, puffy and genial, was manager,
and, some few remember him, an indifferent clown ; his
widow is Mrs. Edouin-Bryer, erstwhile Lizzie Naylor, and
their daughter is Lizzie Bryer. John, only good as
harlequin,Wedded Tilly Earl, but Eliza Edouin, genius of
the family, died ere reaching womanhood, and Rose took
her place, becoming a leading performer and manager,
even as Willie is in London. Julia was petite and piquant,
and the writer well remembers how he was embarrassed
once through having to kiss her, on the amateur stage, in
“ Time Tries All.”

Vague rumours about another revolution
brewing in Hayti. The population of this cheer-
ful State consists of a large number of assorted
niggers, a lot of mulattoes, and some whites, and
they run the Government turn-about. As soon as
the niggers have set up a Cabinet the whites
revolushe, and whenever the whites have started
an administration the niggers revolushe. If the
mulattoes get up anything both the other parties
revolushe. The Dictator generally leans up
against a post in the main street of the capital
with his head in a water-melon, and the Ministers
of State angle for catfish on the wharf. Some-
times there are as many as six Dictators at once,
and they have unseemly scuffles in the palace and
try to choke each other in the doorways. The
boss Dictator for the time being prints all the
paper money, and keeps no accounts. Hayti had
one period of transient prosperity, when a man
nearly seven feet high got.into power and remained
there for eight years, but one morning there was
the usual revolution, and since then all has been
general wreck.
