At a recent pony meeting a certain stable de-
cided to back their pony No. 1, but, not being able to ge >
a fair price, put their money- some £3oo—on their nexs
best, No. 2, at a long shot. The “stiffening ot No. 1,
however, was scarcely satisfactory. So soon as the stable
money went on No. 2, No. 1 went back to a low price,
whena jockey, also riding a pony in the race, thought ne
saw a good investment for a fiver, so put it on No. 1.
Approaching home No. 2 appeared to be winning com-
fortably, but the jockey who had backed No. 1 kept his
mount alongside that animal, and when the time armed
brought his whip down one, two, three on No. 1 s rump,
and before the latter’s jockey could understand matters
he had passed No. 2.and won by a head. Fact—as evi-
denced by the three stinging whip-marks which No. i
carried home on a spot where bis own jockey could not
possibly have reached him,
# * *
Davies, the first bookmaker honoured as “the
Leviathan ”—a title divided in Melbourne between Joe
Thompson and Billy Slack—was a journeyman London
caruenter, but in five years made £140,000, inventing
the list system at hotels. He shelled out £40,000 over one
race, all loss. The biggest bet on record was between
Lords Glasgow and G. Bentinck, £90,000 to ±30,000.
Both deserved to die in the gutter, but such people
never do.
* * *
The death of jockey Roger Davis recalls, after
many years, a racing incident—at present running in
mutilation through the Australian country press—wlueli
is worthy of authentic record. Twenty years ago Colonel,
a favourite horse of the late Mr. Robert Frost, of Orange
(N.S.W.) was matched at Dubbo to race Mr. Tarrant s
mare, Barmaid, from l)nbbo to Orange, a distance of 10c
miles, for £loo' aside, and in the early morn of the
appointed day the pair were started in front of the Dubbo
Post-office,-Davis riding Colonel, and Walter Hiokinbotham,,
of Carbine fame, taking Barmaid for the journey. After
passing Wellington, Davis took the Molong route, Hickm-
botham going vid Ironbarks, and at half-past four o clock
p,m,—nine and a-half hours after starting Colonel gal-
loped through Orange streets and pulled up for the first
time since starting in front of his owner’s residence in
Anson-street. Then followed a unique excitement that is
still unforgotten. The Orange I’ost-offioe, the appointed
winning-post, was round the corner a block away ; as the
horses had taken different routes, Barmaid s actual where-
abouts was unknown, but hoof-beats on the metal were
heard in the distance, and Colonel, having come 100 miles
to his own stable was not inclined to pass it, plunging and
rearing at each effort made to urge him on to the goal.
Another hundred yards would end the race, but ten
minutes of anxious time were passed before he yielded to
Davis’ perseverance and made a record that remains un-
beaten. Barmaid knocked up at Shepherd’s Creek, 30
miles from Orange, and did not reach that town until next
dav. Colonel was in such heart at the finish that his
owner offered to wager that Davis could ride him to
Bathurst, 35 miles further, in three hours without dis-
mounting, and two days after he himself took him to ride
to The owner, trainer and rider of the horse that
performed this memorable feat have all since passed
awav. The trainer, Charlie Bennett, died many years
ao-o ; the spirited owner, one of the whitest of white men,
had his honourable life ended by a fall from a horse in
sight of his own estate, Campdale, just three years ago ,
and flower Davis, the rider, was done to death at last
Molong° races. The gallant horse died in one of the pad-
docks of the estate 12 months after the death of the master
he had faithfully carried for many years.
* * *
About the saddest spectacle we know of is to be
found on the racecourse. We have watched it often, and
it makes us feel bad every time. The spectacle we allude
to is the horse-owner who is trying to look glad on an
up-hill track. His animal is a hot favourite for a big
race, and the public has laid everything on it right up to
its hair and its brains, and then the “books have squared
the proprietor, and he has made it right with the
iookey. He stands to win a tremendous sum it his horse
loses, and to win a lot more if another horse gets in
first • and just when everything is comfortable and
happy he sees something before his staring and in-
furiated head that makes him think lie must be dead and
in Gehenna —it is his thrice-accursed horse winning the
race in a 'canter after all. There is a wild yell of joy
from the whole meeting, and lie is expected to look as it
he were full of unspeakable happiness-and so he is; he is
as happy as a man who has sat down in the fire. He has
won a £lOOO stake and lost a £lO,OOO swindle, and some
sixty or more unhanged furies come round and congratu-
late'him, and he wants to be a centipede so that he can
kick them with a hundred clawed feet at once. He states
that he is profoundly gratified, and smiles a smile that
looks as if he had swallowed himself, and he thanks
them all in a subdued howl and is full of execration right
down to his boots. And when the jockey who sold him
comes by he has to express his admiration of the way he
won the race, when the thing he really wants is to teal oft
that youth’s head and throw it up into the telegraph-
wires. And then the “winner” travels serenely round
for a while receiving the congratulations of all hisfnends,
and goes away into a lonesome spot behind the refreshment
booth, and screams, and tears up great chunks of fi arth
with his nails.

“Dummy” Mace, pugilist, who was beaten by
Dawson the other evening in Sydney, is, as hit) nickname
implies, a mute. He had plenty of confidence prior to the
battle. “How are you going to get on f asked a friend
of his a dav or two before-hand. Dummy s face brightened
up and lie began to “ shape” at an adjacent circular-saw,
as if to say that he felt game even to'fight that. A Sydney
daily winds up the report with this: It was palpable
that Mace was beaten. . . . Dawson, with a look of
disinclination and sympathy, punched him in the jaw,
&o &c. Memorable phrase !

Though no names are mentioned, “Pendragon,”
in a recent number of the Referee, throws light on a
mystery of the P.R. This is the squaring of the Heenan-
King fight. The Benicia Boy was offered a bribe to take
a dose of poison the day before the fight. He took it but
disgorged the drug. The other side, not to be outwitted,
bribed his second, who did not appeal to the referee
when King was fairly knocked out early in the Amt, for
the simple reason that he had only just dosed Heenan
and was waiting until the poison operated. Heenan soon
became stupid, and King, who had fairly lost the fight,
then gave him an awful drubbing. In 1877 the second
wrote to Bell’s Life that Heenan sold the battle for
£2OOO, but all who knew the writer did not believe him,

Tales of the Old Regime,
No. VII.-The Torture of Jane Applegarth.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The quadrangle in front of officers quar-
iers, Sarah Island ; a bracing, but, to the .naked
skin, a biting frostiness of the air ; sunlight of the
■arly morning brilliancy which diffuses neither
warmth nor shadow, bejewelling with clusters of
ustrous gems the dewy, dilated leaves of a young
jelery-topped pine just inside of the western wall
if the stockade; beyond the high pales, a verdant
aspect which wanted but the lowing kine and the
□ox and hawthorn hedges to convince the spectator
;hat one of the countless beauty-spots of the pure
Me “set in the inviolate sea” bad been trans-
planted to this realm of lawlessness; beyond all to
she west, the yeasty surge with its mournful song,
“compassed within a single note;” to the east,
mute figures of destiny, “a thin red line of
soldiers ; in the centre of the quadrangle, flaming
and sparkling nobly, the Union Jack which
haunts its bravery at a height of 80 feet from the
tapered crown of a pine-bole —and, at the foot of
the flagstaff, the triangles. This is the scene.
And the occasion ? The infliction of punishment
on Jane Applegarth, legally convicted of an
offence against the person of Frederick 1 >. Lankes,
Captain in His Majesty’s - th Regiment of Foot,
Penal Commandant at Macquarie Harbour*, but
actually to be penalised for declining to include
herself in the number of the Captain’s concubines.
“ Wherever flies the British flag, declared Lord
Brougham, in a magnificent peroration which
brought tears to the eyes of his auditors, “ there
rule the immutable principles of British justice.”
* * *
A beat of the drums and a clanging of the
fire-locks as they are brought to the salute
announced the arrival of Captain Bankes. He is in
levee uniform, and the bullioned knots of his sword-
tassels gleam richly and the lucid steel of the
scabbard shines whitelyas he marches towards the
centre of the square. Half-way to the flagstalf
he stops, turns round and faces his quarters. At
least that is what the building was known as
officially, but, under the circumstances, it might
not inappropriately be termed his harem. And,
facing it, what does he do? Does he cast an in*
specting eye over the accoutrements and dressing
of the crimson row which proclaims the kinship of
Sarah Island with Windsor Castle and the Houses
of Parliament at Westminster? Not he ; British
Institutions are great, but Love is mightier. Cap-
tain Bankes flicks from the tips of his dogskin
gloves a kiss to a lady who peers curiously through
the open window of the Captain’s She
is late of rising this pleasant morning is Fanny
Cockburn (21), per ship Duke of Wellington, No.
518, and rather than lose the prospect of the
method by which a due perception of the
omnipresence of British Justice is to be engrafted
in Jane Applegarth’s blood (Jane was No. 513
by the same vessel) Fanny, Concubine-in-Chief,
appears at the window in a negligee costume.
Though her shoulders are not bare, they are so
thinly-clad by their laced frippery, that she
shudders slightly as the breath of the morning
nips them. She observes the Captain-Com-
mandant’s salute, and prettily returns it the same
instant as she calls to Perry, her fellow-transport
but lesser light of the seraglio per the Duke, to
bring her a warm wrap.
The care with which the Commandant furnished
the wardrobe of his ladies was only equalled by
his attentiveness to the duty of seeing that their
garments were properly paid for out of the Settle-
ment’s accounts. The stock of men’s shirts was
always deficient compared with the time record,
but the System was so perfectly organised that the
loss on that item was made up by a surplus of
ladies’ lingerie. Three “ration” shirts would
always purchase a finely-frilled chemise, and
though the male-convicts to whom the former
“ were issued ” never received the coarse things,
they would, doubtless, have grown warm with an
unselfish glow had they reflected that their shirts’
equivalent,, the finely-frilled chemise, was worn on
the chastely-curved shoulders of Fanny Cockburn
and those of her predecessors and successors.
# # *
The Captain, still in the first glamour of
an unholy love, is rejoiced at his Fanny’s return-
kiss, and, like a knight of the old chivalrous ages
who fought the harder when benisoned by his
ladye-love’s smile, he rushes the more impetuously
into the fray. In other words, he is so fortified
by the assuring glance of the principal of his fair
ones, that while he has entered the quadrangle
with a shrinking fear of what Convict Jane
Applegarth will say to him before the men of the
guard, he is now determined to go through the
whole punishment-routine, and to set the woman
at defiance. She has a pointed tongue has Jane,
and he has already writhed under it, but now he
is resolved, dauntless, and immoveable—in the
interests of Justice.
Thus the scene; now for the action.
* * *
“ Bring out the prisoner,” Bankes ordered.
And Jane Applegarth, 513, per Duke of Welling-
ton, was ushered from the cells. Behind her step
three or four warders and the sconrger. The last
is to be known to the present generation by the
cognomen of Wiry Ned. It cannot be said that
the appellation was peculiarly appropriate, looking
at his build, for, if anything, he was, notwith-
standing the superabundant exercise which the
System required him to take, grossly-bulky. James
Backhouse, the Quaker Missionary, wrote of the
punishment returns of two or three years later
that the number of lashes inflicted on 188
prisoners was 0280. Of course the good Friend
went solely by the records, and as Macquarie
Harbour Pumshment-book must have been a
model of the System’s unveracity (unless men like
Commissariat-Officer Lempriere and Under.
Sheriff Crouch lied, the average weekly “dose
would furnish good exercise to the official flogger.
When Backhouse knew him after he had wielded
many more round thousands of cuts he was still
fleshy, so we may presume he was peculiarly well
supplied with adipose tissue on this refreshing
morning. Why, then, he was called Wiry, is a
mystery, unless it was that ho was fond of assert-
ing that “he didn’t believe in no copper-wire
crackers to his cats, like Loo’ten’t Darrell, for
he’d lay Ms ’cad he’d pickle a man as neatly wi’out
th’ wire as wi’ it.” It was this connoisseur in
“cats” to whom Jane Applegarth was to be
handed over for what the historian Kusden is
accustomed to mildly term eccentric punish-
merits.”
Inoo V\ e - gir V P • J” arl^ 8, .
Dickson ” (this to the burgeon) there is no
reason why the prisoner should not be punished.
A squat figure, with eyes that spoke eloquently
of a long over-night debauch, stepped from behind
the line of the soldiers, felt Applegarth’s pulse,
bent its head to the girl’s bosom to listen to the
beating of her heart, parted with flippant fingers
the eyelids which would have shrouded the shame-
stricken gaze, and said —
“ Oh, she’s strong enough, sir, and can take her
c l o se.”
Wiry Ned laid his hand upon the girl’s shoulder
anc j pushed her with more show of rudeness than
was the fact towards the triangles. She moved for-
ward mechanically to the punishment-machine —
an q t be Union Jack.
Bankes followed her with his eyes, and then, as

WRITWRIGHT (the Melbourne Solicitor): “Reprieve Johnston, sir? Absurd! Besides, I’m
promised six tickets for the execution, and the duty of the Crown is clear.”
she neared the whipping-post, saw a ray of the
new sunlight flash from one of the legs of the
accursed tripod. The shiny appearance of the
wood struck him as being singular. He put up
his eye-glass, looked at it a second time, and then
expressed a laudable curiosity to know what was
the cause of that glinting.
“ What on earth have you been doing, Seourger,
to the triangles ? What makes ’em shine so this
morning?”
Ned turned round with a grin ornamenting the
flabbiness which hid his criminal facial angle,
“ Oh, Comd’nant, I tho’t as how this bein’ a ’tic’ler
’kashun, I’d rub ’em up a bit, so I polished ’em
wi’ sand.”
Ob, the gay laugh that rippled from the upper
window, to melt into the boisterous merriment of
Captain Bankes and Surgeon Dickson. The girl
Cockburn thought the idea so funny !
* * *
Jane was triced up, and Captain Bankes
bethought himself as he was strong he would be
merciful. He would give the hardened criminal
at the triangle (not the choice of punishments—
they were already fixed by the Magistrate) but of
the order of her punishment.
“Pris’ner,” he exclaimed, an august condescen-
sion lending a pleasing tone to his voice. “ Will
you be shaved first or have the flogging first ? ”
She turned her head upon her shoulders and
looked at him. Nevertheless, answer she made
not, save for the look of stinging contempt with
which she scanned him.
Up to this time she had worn undisturbed her
upper garment. Possibly it was thoughtlessness
on Wiry Ned’s part that had permitted him to
allow her to retain unviolated by his hand the
flimsy thing which guarded her modesty from the
rude eyes whose slightest glance was an insult.
From what followed, however, we are inclined to
think that it was not thoughtlessness, but thought-
fulness. The look of contempt with which the
prisoner favoured the Commandant unloosed, so
to speak, that bodice. Officer—and Gentleman—
Bankes could not but resent the girl’s scorn, and
sharply he called Wiry Ned to order.
“Oh, you won’t speak, won’t you, you jade?
You talked too much before, and now your game
is silence, is it ? We'll fix the progression of events.
What do you say, Doctor?- shall we give her the
cat first? And by Seourger, why haven’t you
stripped her ?” . . ~
The Doctor was prompt in answering : W ell,
I should say, sir, shave her first, then flog her,
and then ”
Jane spoke now. This time, however, she flung
her words, with another look of vitriolic derision,
at the doctor. “And brand her,” she said, com-
pleting his sentence.
The Seourger was almost as ready as the Doctor
to speak. He had coached himself in his line of
speech and action, and said: “I didn’t think as
’ow yer’d want it, Com’dant —my leetle tools ’ll do
the work so well. They’ll bring the dress off with
the tail, sir, each stroke. Just yer see now, sir
—and the beggar affected a chuckle. Following
his words he stooped down, apparently to throw
round the limb another bight of the cord which
fastened Jane’s ankle to the cross-bar—he had so
far respected her sex that he did not “ spread-
eagle ” her, and had linked the two legs of the
tripod by a piece of pine-batten —and while thus
stooping he bade her in a whisper “not to be
afeerd ! I ain’t agoin’ to nick yer on’y a codger,”
The girl bowed her head. She heard the whis-
pered words, though as the Scourger’s humorous
reference to the notion he said he had of bringing
away her dress and her skin in the one stroke had
distended the laughter-loving mouth of the
medical man, it would not have been surprising
had she failed to learn that the Scourger’s
sympathies were to be directed in her direction.
The ankle-knot having been adjusted, the Wiry
’Un rose to his full height, saluted, and informed
the Commandant that he was ready.
“Got the shears?” queried the Commandant.
A leather-cased bundle lay at the far foot of the
triangles. In a second of time the Seourger had
the packet untied, and its contents exposed to the
gaze of everyone on, or overlooking, the square.
Some of the' articles that were in it fell clanking
to the gravel; they were not wanted just
yet; they were not yet red-hot; their function
was an hour in the future. But the “cat ” and the
shears were for almost immediate use.
“ Yes, sir.”
“ Then shave her.”
Wiry Ned in after years became rather a
favourite of Parson Marsden’s—whose admiring
contemporaries of tener spoke of him as “Old Flog-
>em by whom Ned was made an overseer at
Parramatta, and the ladies fair who there visited
the Factory for the purpose of being “ barbered,”
were, as often as not, dealt with by the ex-scourger
of the Harbour. Seldom in those Parramatta days
(so the Wiry Ned used to say, at least) had it ever
been his good fortune to handle and to scissor so
beautiful a mass of tawny hair as dropped to the
ground from Jane Applegarth’s head. It is a
digression, but we may as well remark that, when
Ned sold a batch one day of convict-women’s hair
to Foss, the chemist, of 18 Pitt Street, Sydney,
predecessor to John Tawell, Quaker and murderer,
he lamented that ho had never kept Jane’s hair.
“Lor’, Mr. Ambrose,” he said, “she’s a well-to-
do woman now, I b’heve, but she ain’t got no ’air
to equal that wot I snipt off ’er at the ’arbour nine
year ago. Lor’, sir, I’d ’a wanted a yellow-boy an
ounce for that wig of her’n.”
As it lay on the Macquarie Harbour soil,
there being no local market for such rubbish,
Jane Applegartlrs tawny mane was left to
be tossed by the pitying breeze, and to be
trodden upon by brutish feet. As the shears— ’
did we make it quite clear to you that the
Wiry ’Un was also Settlement barber—touched
her scalp and neck with their cold rims, she
shuddered, and looked piteously at the tonsorial
artist. The glory of her sex fell from her, it
seemed, as the locks shimmered through the sun-
shine in their descent.
“ Ready, sir ! ” cried Ned, as he stepped back
and ‘ ‘ combed ” the cats with the fingers of his
left hand, and nursed a stray strand back into its
place. “Ready, sir!”
The Doctor stepped forward, and looked at the
Commandant. The Commandant looked at the
Doctor —and nodded. And the Doctor said,
Now ! ”
# * *
The cats swirled, and the body of the
woman took a half-twist. But it must have been
from the shame of the blow, and not from its pain.
A strip of dress came away —no skin.
“ One ! ” cried the Doctor. In most settlements
the principal constable tallied the cuts, but at the
Harbour it was a privilege—may we hope a highly
esteemed one—of the medical men.
“ Two ! ” Another strip of the checked stuff
fell from the cats—still no skin; neither, any
blood.
“Three!” “Four!” “Five!” —the blows
fell quickly—too quickly to please the gentlemen
of the Old Regime. Of what value was punitive
discipline, if the Scourger was to make things light
by hitting quickly, when the slower blows inflicted
more pain ?
Thus thinking, Duty—“ stern daughter of the
voice of God ’’—bade the doctor interfere.
“ Here. I say, scourger ! What are you up to ?
You are codgering, man.” The subordinate
official of the Regime thereupon put a little more
vim into his next blow, but took the opportunity,
though, of making a slip in his step, so bringing
himself for the moment close to Jane. “ I’ll ’ave
to give yer jest one, me lass, so toe your chalk ! ”
By which whisper the treacherous fellow conveyed
his wish that she should brace herself for the one
or two torturing cuts that he felt called upon—in
the interests of one Wiry Ned, Scourger—to
administer. He might successfully “codger,”
say, 18 blows out of 20 ; but, if, by the time the
score was tallied, no reddish-blue wheals were im-
printed on her flesh, then by one of those sudden
transitions for which the System was famous, and
through which the hangman often become the
hanged and the scourger the scourged, he might
himself be the tied up to the tripod. Which was
not a pleasant thing to contemplate. And—so—
and so
Even Fanny Cockburn, concubine -in - chief,
clapped her hands to her ears as “Eight!’’was
tallied, and “ Nine !” was tallied, and the tally of
ten was called.
* # *
The doctor was by no means satisfied that
the second, and final, tally of ten was as well-
executed or not—as the first. He was satisfied
that the flogger had been “codgering,” and,
generally, making things easy for the floggee.
“ You’ve been codgering, Scourger !”
“ Beg parding, Doctor Dickson, I did me best,
s’elp me, sir ! Jest look at them stripes !” He
pointed with the cat-handle to a couple of weals
which thrust their vicious colour through the rent
.checks of the bodice. “ They all fell there, sir.
I ’adn’t no borders to mark ’er all hover, doctor.
Doctor Dickson was too old an officer to be
deceived by such reasoning. “Those are the
stripes of the first tally ? How do I know you hit
her the second tally at all ? You d d fellows
have almost as many tricks with the cat as you ve
had strokes with it yourselves.” ,
Ned protested his innocence of any design to
defraud the System. He called the Confidant and
other deities to witness he had “ ’it square•
Did they not hear the flesh “ sw-sw-oqge” at eacli
and every stroke? He would forfeit his soul
nay, even that precious privilege for which he had
oft bartered that perjured atom—his weekly dole
of ’baccy—if he could be proved to be speaKin o
falsely! i

person present who could speak positively to tn

character. She records :
Edward Whipps, alias Wiry Ned, alias John Ripstone,
4732 per Neptune, failure to inflict punishment as ordered

The Muse of History occasionally makes slips.
The Harbour was a “ male ” penal settlement —no
women were supposed to be there; there is no
strictly official record of the women ever getting
there; no woman-transport was, in that en-
lightened day, held to be liable to flogging except
by the hand of woman ; and yet, in a
moment of forgetfulness, she permitted
this entry to be made by the hands of
the very wretch whoso peculiar interest it was
that the proceeding should be forgotten as soon as
perpetrated! Really, sometimes, the devil does
not take care of his own. And the Clean are not
always fo.
How the Wiry ’Un cursed those languishing
syes of the pretty Cockburn girl, which could see
so keenly, when he promptly followed Jane at the
triangles ! For the scourger, pro tern., had been
under his hands the month previous, and the
scourger, pro tern., had, oh, a remarkably good
memory, and such a lively vengeance !
“ There wasn't any funking over that dose, was
there, Ned ?’ laughingly interrogated the doctor,
is the flogger, pro tern., having gone leisurely
through Ned’s “dozen,” once more retired into
private life and the cell whence he had emerged
into temporary officialdom. .
“ No, sir. There wasn’t! Thank you, sir !”
“ Don't mention it, my man—you’re quite wel-
come !” And the doctor laughed at his own
aumour. Which wasn’t his humour at all; it was
Mr. D’Arcy Wentworth’s, of Old Sydney.
* # *
If Wiry Ned was nipped to the bones by
the cold air as he stood shirtless to receive his
punishment, it was not long before he was in a
position to warm himself— and Jane.
She had, upon her release from the triangles,
oeen permitted to stand unmolested for twenty
minutes or so - fo stand in the cold, biting atmos-
phere—to stand, with the agonised eyes of a dumb
animal in the trap—to stand, with cheeks that
now paled to the whiteness of the spumy surf,
and now flamed to the scarlet of the thin, red
line; and wait for what came next.
* * *
The brands were “next!” You will re-
member the settlement’s branding-irons? They
were
\ oii =
Heated in the tinsmith’s brazier, those irons, or
such of them as were necessary, were successively
impressed upon the gleaming white of her shoulders.
Above the welt that signified Ned’s devotion to
the duty of his scourgership, her flesh crinkled,
and crackled, and singed, as, with all the haste he
could, the Wiry Ned affixed first one iron and then
another till these outlines were marked on tho
outraged body :
W, 513.
Consider that the process took, for the “ W.,”
four stampings; for the “5,” three stampings;
and should have taken for the other figures one
and two respectively. And consider, further,
that the Wiry ’Un made a mistake in impressing
the “1,” because he took up the shorter iron
instead of the longer, and thus had to repeat the
stamp to make the height of the figures uniform.
Consider these things, and also the circumstance
that even at Macquarie Harbour branding with
hot irons was an “unusual” punishment, and
that, therefore, Ned was not an expert, and you
will not be surprised to learn that the last brand
burnt into a body that was nearer death than life.
Still, the decision of the Law had to be respected.
* * #
It was in 1878 that a Hobart Town convict,
under sentence of life-imprisonment, sketched for
us on the whitewash of his cell-wall, the rude out-
lines of the Harbour brands, and told us, in
garnished phrase, the gist of the story of Jane
Applegarth, woman-transport. We doubted him
at the time ; although he could neither read nor
write; we doubted him (your convict truth-
speaker was generally an illiterate); only when,
years afterwards, we bought, in the old bookshop
opposite the ship Jane, Hobart-town, a dim,
stained manuscript bearing an autograph yet to be
esteemed precious in our Australian archives, did
we recall the prisoner’s story, and believe it.
And the letter “W” on Jane Apple-
garth’s flesh ? Cannot you guess why Com-
mandant Bankes stamped her with that supreme,
that indelible insult ? It was because she was the
only woman at the Settlement who did not
deserve it!
And Jane Applegarth herself? We told you; a
week or two ago, what happened to her and to
Bankes, when, after her torture, he caused her
to be removed to Limeburners’ Point —to the hut
—and the embraces —of the lime-gang. We have
reason to think she met Fanny Cockburn in later
life, under very different circumstances.
PRICE WARUNG.
POLITICAL
POINTS
W. J. Lyne, M.P., states that 2000 persons
in Sydney have 12 Parliamentary votes each.
London Punch seriously suggests that half-
pay officers should be employed as criminal
jurors.
It is believed that the current year will
increase the public debt of N.S.W. to nearly ;>0
millions,
“The sense of Property turns sand to
gold,” says a writer, trying to controvert Henry
George.
Abigail says he is favourable to Federation
on fair terms but will not swallow the Common-
wealth Bill as a whole.
Buenos Ayres Standard informs us that,
with a population of under 4,000,000, the Argen-
tine Republic lias a debt of £93,000,000 !
Parkes, finding that he can neither coerce
Ins colleagues nor the public, lias determined to
make the Federation question an open one.
COLONIAL BATTLES.
FAIR VISITOR (at encampment); “This is what you call warfare, is it? Why, theres
nothing to show where your battles are fought! ”
O FFICER: “Pardon me, we can always show lots of “ Dead Marines!”
Trades Unionism is described as a decay-
ing symbol of a past life. Socialism, however,
is fitly alleged to be the germs of a new existence.
The Ballarat militia have been indulging
in a mutiny. As nearly all the men were in it,
the law will have to shake its head solemnly, and
pass on.
On a rough count of noses, the supporters
of the Federal Constitution Bill are in an utterly
bopele.-s minority in both houses of the N.S.W.
Legislature.
The policy of Parkes’ men-colleagues in
the N.S.W. Cabinet as against those of his mice-
colleagues and tools, is to hang the Commonwealth
Bill up to dry.
Labouchere’s tirades against the useless-
ness of colonies to the “mother country” were
probably inspired by Oliver Goldsmith, in bis
“ Citizen of the World.”
Convention Delegate Hackett, of W.A.,
writes that that province is up to date either
apathetic or hostile on the subject of Federation.
However, he has hopes.
The Labour Party returned their two can-
didates for the S.A. Legislative Council, and
between them polled almost as many as did the
other seven candidates collectively.
Premier Playford (S.A.) returned to Ade-
laide from the Melbourne Conference more firmly
convinced than ever that nothing will come until
one-man-one-vote is secured in all the Australian
provinces.
Melbourne Tailoresses’ Society donates £lO
to Queensland shearers, as a thanksgiving for
being delivered from Mr. Giltgore. It remains
for the domestic servants to emulate the snip-
peresses.
The Bulletin will bet any politician a
black coat and a black belltopper, also a white
waistcoat and a big watch-chain, that Australia
will not be federated save on the basis of one-
man-one-vote.
Premier Munro evidently did not forge* 3
the Cabbage Garden or the “Dead as Juliu 8
Caesar ” episodes, for Victoria was about the only
Australasian province which did not send a floral
tribute to Sir John Robertson’s funeral.
The Jews are not expelled from Czardom
under any new law, but under a time-honoured one
which declares that no .lew shall be allowed in
Great Russia. The Russian Jews, three millions,
number about half the globular total.
Victorian Census returns show that the
Rodney district, which diminished in population
between ’7l and ’Bl, developed great fecundity
under the political sway of member Gillies.
Probably Duncan fed his constituents on the fat
of the land.
Currently reported that the various Aus-
tralian Governments have arrived at an under-
standing on the Labour difficulty. Looking at
the materials of which these Governments are
composed, it does not require a prophet to fore-
tell the bent of their mutuality.
Captain Verney, M.P., who pleaded guilty
to procuring a young girl for immoral purposes,
lias, it's cabled, got twelve months’ gaol. If he
had pleaded guilty to forging a cheque for £lO he
would have got five years as surely as that the sun
rises. “Property ! property ! ”
As showing the rapacity of land-owners,
even when the State is benefiting them, it is an-
nounced that in connection with a recently-
authorised N.S.W. railway, 52 claims were pre-
sented to Government for resumed land
amounting to £30,000. The amount ultimately
awarded was only £5OOO !
No, the exiled Russian Jews must not
come in a mob to Australia, which country recog-
nises that its urban population is already hugely
in excess of its rural. No further immigration of
the artisan class even is desirable. One salutary
lesson the Jews might teach the Australian work-
men—how to stick together.
Squatter Palmer, in retiring from the
Queensland Acting-Governorship eulogises the
services of the military in re the present labour
troubles. Squatter Palmer lately swore that he
could not ride round his estate in three days, or
eight days, or something. Under the circum-
stances, it is easy to enthuse about the military.
At the banquet given by the Ballarat
Trades and Labour Council to the labour
delegates, when the toast of “the Queen ” was pro-
posed, about 40 of the visitors remained seated.
When the toasters sat down, Delegate Macdonald,
of Queensland, leaped on his chair, and shouted
‘Not the Queen, the People!” and carried the
crowd with him.
The British Commons are expected to dis-
solve shortly. Is England going once more to
justify 7, Bisn arck’s saying, that there is a complete
veer round of the political weathercock every six
years : Gladstone 18G8—74, Disraeli 1874 —80,
Gladstone 1880—6, Salisbury 1886—92? Of course
it is Liberalism that gains, with what Christopher
North calls the “spangs” of the tiger.
Sydney will not be the federal Capital
of the Commonwealth. Tasmania, South Aus-
tralia and Victoria would vote for Melbourne ;
Queensland and N.S. Wales for Sydney. It could
be claimed no doubt that Sydney, in time to come,
with the development of Northern and Western
Australia, would be the most central large city.
But then, you see, “ the time to come ’ has not yet
arrived.
Peacock ana Kirton, two members of the
Victorian Native party, accompanied the Female
Suffrage deputation that waited upon Premier
Munro last week. The young men and matured
married women seem warmer than the old men
and maidens on this great question. This is un-
fortunate. As the young men take wives and
grow old the maidens will become married and
matured, thus preserving the balance of opinion
as at present.
The Pope’s latest encyclical on the labour
question “ denounces excessive capitalistic de-
mands and the unjust claims of the Socialists,
and holds that the strained relations between
capital and labour throughout Europe demand
State intervention, otherwise the labour problem
will remain for ever unsolved. Leo partially
grasps the situation. The labour party, by their
action in preparing for fair representation in the
legislatures, are now busy in arranging that the
State sha 1 l interfere.
We read that “Northumberland (N.fcl.W.)
is to have two working labour candidates nomin-
ated next election, and consequently there will be
a severe fight, as the three present members,
Messrs. Melville, Walker, and Greer will stand.”
The Labour Party can easily do without Mr. Joey
Greer, but The Bulletin would advise it to care-
fully consider the expedient of withdrawing sup-
port from Melville and Walker, who, so far as we
know, have been absolutely straight on all demo-
cratic questions.
From the Age (last week) :
In a letter received by a Presbyterian clergyman at
Ballarat from the private secretary of the Governor it is
stated that his Excellency will not be influenced by the
petition which is being prepared in favour of the con-
demned man, Johnston, and that the execution will
accordingly be allowed to take place on the date fixed.
That a Governor’s private secretary should write
such a letter is almost incredible, considering that
the Governor personally is the final court of
appeal, and that the Imperial instructions to
him are to hear the advice of his Ministers
but to act on his omi responsibility alone. The
Governor is never at liberty, until a criminal has
been hanged, to say that he is beyond the reach of
argument respecting the condainne’s case.
Dear Bulletin, —Allow me to make a
suggestion with regard to the future Federal
Capital of Australia. To avoid any jealousy or
ill-feeling between the peoples of existing towns
or cities, let us build the Capital in the wilderness
in a position picturesque and grand. Let it be a
dream of architectural beauty worthy of our
glorious country. It should be a model for future
surveyors and builders of cities, as we hope to
show the way to the nations in progressive and
enlightened legislation. As a balm to Repub-
lican feeling, as an incentive to rnaniy and noble
actions, and as a tribute of respect to the memory
of our most earnest and practical patriot, let us
call it—What? — Yours, Orb. Oakleigh, Vic.
Extract, Sec. 8, U.S.A. Constitution,
Article 1 to bo read in connection with our first
leading-article of to-day :
The Congress shall have power—
To raise and support armies, but no appropriation of
money to that use shall be for a longer term than two
years.
To provide and maintain a navy.
To make rules for the Government and regulation of
the land and naval forces.
To provide for calling forth the militia to execute the
laws of the Union, suppress insurrections, and repel in-
vasions.
To provide for organising, arming, and disciplining the
militia, and for governing such parts of them as may be
employed in the service of the United States, reserving to
the States respectively the appointment of the officers, and
the authority of training the militia according to the dis-
cipline pi'escribed by Congress.
1 he flock of Melbourne ladies who flut-
tered round Jimmy Munro and chirped for the
suffrage was unable to keep off the grovel paths.
References to the revered occupant of the British
Throne crop up wherever a few petticoats are
gathered together, and the awful “loyalty ” of the
advanced female always manages to assert itself
somehow. One of James’ petitioners said that
“ woman’s capability intellectually was provf d by
her now having a position among doctors, lawyers,
pastors; by her university degrees, and by her
reigning in grace and wisdom as Queen of the
realm.” This last reason for giving votes to the
fair sex is sweetly illogical, because British
Queens date back to the days of Boadicea, and
there was a Bloody Mary at the head of affairs
at a time when women knew as much about
the law and senior wranglerships as a cow knows
about cannon-balls.
Premier Munro last week told a deputa-
tion who advocated woman-franchise, that “if the
extension were limited to rate-payers there would
be very little difficulty.” Delicious, this! A
lurid light is hero cast upon the real political con-
victions of the democratic Munro, one of the
makers of the Commonwealth Bill. He favours
one-man-one-vote, which lie sees is inevitable,
and yet he talks with complacency of set-
ting up another Tory class —of proper-
tied and well-to do women. The woman who
has to earn her own living comparatively rarely
has uroperty or pays rates. Wo are going
to have one-man-one-vote shortly and when
woman-suffrage comes along we shall enfranchise
every woman over the age of 21 years, whether she
be a retired publicaness, living in gilded splendour
at Toorak or Potts’ Point, or a twister in a tobacco-
factory. The Lord defend ns against a female
politically-privileged class !

Society
“George Atkinson” writes to Sydney
D.T., apropos to the verdict passed upon the
“Young Lady of the Period” by Mark Guy
Pearse :
If I had been at Mr. Pearse’s lecture he would have
been instantly challenged by myself to produce in Eng-
land or elsewhere five females more competent in all
household duties than my own five daughters, born in
Sydney city.
After this good reclame, the end of the five Misses
Atkinson, as Misses, should not bo far off. It is
not often a man has such a chance of directly
advertising his gals. What price this style ?
-> * #
Amoosin’ little paragraph :
Lady Parkes, who has been indisposed for some days,
was slightly better yesterday. Lord Jersey on hearing
of her ladyship’s illness forwarded a letter of sympathy
to Sir Henry Parkes.
Either Old Man Parkes or Governor Jersey is a
better and more persistent advertiser than even
Mr. Atkinson. Apropos, which of the pair is the
awful snob who communicates such items to the
press !

It is whispered that a Sydney medico,
professionally acquainted with about half the
young men of N.S. Wales, is coming out on abso-
lutely democratic lines at next general election.
* * *
The election of Fitzgibbon to the Chair-
manship of the new Melbourne Board of Works
is a great blow to the Sir M. H. Davies’ clique,
whose nominee, Muntz, brother of the Birming-
ham Muntz, considered his selection cocksure,
thanks to the ramifications, pecuniary and social,
of Speaker and Syndicator Davies. Muntz was
one of Davies’ colleagues (lots of people use a
less complimentary term) in Prahran “City”
Council, which was the second stage —the first
was the Y.M.C.A. —to Davies’ “honours.” Be-
tween the Munro-Building-Sooiety-and-Banking-
clique and the Davies’ ditto-ditto-ditto there is a
bitter feud, all the stronger that it is disguised
’neath the brotherly love of the Saints, The
opportunity given to the Munro crowd of bestow-
ing a back-hander on the Speaker’s crew, per
medium of nonsensical old Fitz., was, therefore,
not to be lost by any means.
« * #
A particularly strong man was wanted for
the Chairmanship of a Metropolitan Board of
Works in Melbourne—and poor old Fitzgibbon’s
nomination has been made in utter disregard of
the public need. There could be little doubt that
the appointment of the bumptious boss of the
Melbourne City Council is a sop to the city
proper, and is a step which cannot help (Fitz.’s
antecedent views of suburban municipalities
being borne in mind) but prove disastrous to the
development of the outlying suburbs. The
appointment is a radically bad one. Fitz. has
seen his best days and done his best work long
ago, and as the executive action of the new Board
will rest mainly with him, the suburban members
will, from the outset, have to waste their time and
energy in fighting Fitz. ’s ineradicable tendency to
build up a monstrous centralisation, instead of
concentrating both on the work of the Board.
* # #
A Maoriland young lady was engaged to .
an apparently prosperous manufacturer in another
city, when, a few days before the date fixed for
their marriage, news was received that his firm
had become insolvent. A letter was at once
written and posted to the allegedly penniless
Romeo breaking the match off, but, ere the mail
had left, a telegram arrived from a relative stating
that rumour had lied. The weeping maid and her
parents swooped down on the post-office demand-
ing the letter’s return, but the official rule
admitted of no exception, and now the- blighted
bride is biting her lips, while the saved lover is
thanking his stars for a lucky escape.

We cannot help smiling when we read of
a certain N.S. W. Stipendiary giving six months’
to an habitual drunkard.

Quack doctors, remarks a talented Bul-
letin man, are so called because they make all
their money out of the little ducks.
* # #
The Melbourne Corporation is beginning
to recognise that one of the sexes, at all events —
Man —is subject to depletion as well as absorption.
As for the poor women, it is this way : Byron
could not bear to see them eat or drink, and other
people will not believe they do anything else.
* # *
A dozen Chinese laundries can now be
counted in Melbourne. One Chow has his work-
shop in Russell-street in the front room of what
used to be a private house, and a gaping crowd
can generally be seen roaring with laughter at
John as he irons the shirts and flattens out the
women’s underwear.
Apropos , a squatter who, with his wife and
family, came down for a trip to Melbourne, put
up at a swell city hotel and entered upon the
pursuit of mild enjoyment. Passing down
Russell-street one evening, en route for the
Alexandra, the husband and wife were astonished
to hear the lady’s full name derisively shouted out
by a crowd of larrikins congregated round a
dimly-lighted window. Pushing towards the
tenement, and peering through the dirty gauze
curtains, the lord of several thousand head of
stock beheld a sprightly young Chinaman in full
dress, and cigarette en louche, hanging up a petti-
coat which certainly belonged to “the missus,”
and which he had evidently just ironed. He
then, with a grin, proceeded to grab a white vest
of the in-gazer’s, whereupon the alert mob once
more burst into a Greek chorus, while his wife
straggled frantically to obtain a view of the busi-
ness. Without a word of explanation the unhappy
man tore her away and hustled her home. An
alteration in the hotel laundry-department has
since been effected.
* * *
Dr. Bride, Melbourne Public Librarian,
was among the slain in the contest for the Chair-
manship of the Melbourne Board of Works. His
candidature was a marvel to many people, as he
was looked upon as a scholar, pure and simple,
whose heart was a bibliographical treatise,
delicately bound in superfine Russia, French-
tooled, gilt over red edges. The proceeding, how-
ever, is no surprise to those who know what Bride
has had to contend with, between his trustees on
the one hand, and the Public Service Commis-
sioners on the other. In the preparation of his
admirably-devised card-catalogue, which pro-
mises to be the superlative work of the kind
within Great Britain and her “ Dependencies ”

—he has had the assistance of practically nobody
except a lot of untrained cubs whose knowledge
of literature is made up by Matriculation papers,
“Jack Harkaway,” and “The Bold Bandit of
Bugaboo.” The principal assistants of the Insti-
tution, Dr. Gagliardi, Bowden, 8.A., Armstrong,
8.A., have their time monopolised in the work of
supervision in their several departments, and the
cataloguing, which should have given results of
importance to the whole world of book-lovers, has
been only saved from becoming a mere compila-
tion on the plan of booksellers’ lists by Bride’s love
for and devotion to his work.

The recently-cabled news that the detach-
ment of Victorian Mounted Rifles who have gone
to the English tournament are to be quartered
with the Life Guards at Windsor, is being much
gushed over in the Melbourne papers. To be
quartered with the Royal bodyguard may please
the Victorian Mounted Rifles and Co., but the
Australian democracy regards the alleged com-
pliment with supreme indifference. It views the.
class who now control the Australian military as
the sworn enemies of the people, and will make
things hot for them when Big Black Wednesday
comes along.

The appointment of Mr. Holtzeas Director
of Adelaide Botanic Gardens at £450 per annum
is another instance of relentless cutting-down in
the S.A. Civil Service. The late Dr. Schomburgk
received £6OO for the work, but in order to save
£l5O per annum the Government slices the position
down to a second-hand billet—and at the same
time agree to pay the picnic expenses of a so-
called ‘ * expedition ” by a notoriety-hunting
Governor.
* * *
It was made abundantly evident at the
cricket-match played on the Rushcutters’ Bay
Oval, Sydney, between the I. Zingari and
The Fleet that ladies don’t care a dump
for cricket. From the start they stood with
their umbrageous hats and parasols, and that
charming disregard for manners and other
folks’ convenience, which is woman’s prerogative,
in front of the males who were eager to watch the
game. Towards the close of the afternoon there
were several hundred ladies and not a few gentle-
men congregated in the vicinity of the vice-regal
carpet inside the playing ground, and it is safe to
say that not ten of them had an eye to the game
for a single moment. As Lord Jersey and
Admiral Scott scored three each the question of
precedence may be supposed to remain unsettled
in the latter gentleman’s opinion. Bishop
Saumaurez Smith was in attendance, giving His
Ex. a few points about bo vling. It was suggested
that the Bishop of Yorker would be a good title
for a man of his cricketing attainments.

The monomaniac, Wilson, hanged on Mon-
day for an offence that is no longer punishable by
death “at home,” spent the last fortnight of Ids
life in shrieking, groaning, and beating his head
against the walls of his cell in Ballarat-gaol. The
man’s hopeless lunacy was patent to all th<
officials, who would have regarded the “ turninj
off ” of paralytic Johnston from a stool as a
pleasant spectacle by comparison.

Another expensive, full-orchestra wedding
at Melbourne Anglican Cathedral is in the wind"
Next time there will be a strong body of police
engaged to protect the pot-plants and watch ovei
the prodigal display of cut blossoms. Any Toorak
lady who breaks through the line of defence, and
attempts to carry off a five-guinea orchid undet
cover of a haughty stare, will run a risk of arrest
by one of the plain-clothes officers stationed at
discreet distances within the sacred building.
Several Melbourne florists are fearful of lending
pot-plants on these occasions. They say that a
wedding gets the ladies’ blood up and that when
the female aristocracy do get their first-family
blood fairly up they will grab at anything.
* # #
A story of a very staid and solemn Aus-
tralian M.P. has been given us. Domestic dissen-
sions, mere or less serious, had determined the
“guid wife ” to leave the connubial home, and in
order to prepare for the journey she secured 500
sovereigns and placed them in charge of a feminine
friend. Husband and wife, however, made up
their little differences and agreed to wade through
the balance of life together. The wife immediately
waited upon the “friend,” who, however, had by
some extraordinary lapse of memory forgotten all
about the £5OO, and her memory has continued a
blank to this day. But a certain pub. in an inland
town had a new storey put on it, and the landlady
thereof flourishes.

Lecturing to the N.S.W. Ambulance
Society, before Lady Jersey, Dr. Ellis started off—
“ Since Lady Brassey has come amongst us .”
“ Same old speech !” interjected someone, and it
evidently was the speech delivered when that now
deceased lady founded the society. The mistake
somewhat broke the doctor up, and his equani-
mity was not improved by the sotto voce remark of a
lady who said : “ So like a medical man to resur-
rectpoor Lady Brassey !” Moral : Editing is a
profession.
* * #
In Hay, N.S.W., there is or was a custom
among “the talent” of whistling “Pop goes the
Weasel” whenever anyoneon horseback proceeded
up or down the street, and the police have had
some of the young gentlemen fined for doing so
under the charge of “insulting behaviour.’ A
correspondent asks The Bulletin whether this is
law or merely bush law, and whether a person
can be given in charge for whistling it or any
other air; also if there is no appeal in law against
such a decision. In reply we can only say that if
such whistling is locally regarded as insulting it
is insulting and is punishable by law.
# # #
The N.S. Wales Presbyterian Assembly
publicly plume themselves on the good work done
by the church among the Sydney Chinese. Dr.
Andrew Ross, M.P., points out that in two of the
largest and oldest-settled counties of N.S.W. with
a population of 8000 souls, not a single Pres-
byterian clergyman is on hand to baptise, marry,
or even read the burial service. Molong, the
largest town of his constituency, has a neat Pres-
byterian church but no parson, and the people are
thinking of turning it into a skating-rink.
* # #
Mary Sharpies, of Northcote, in the com-
petition for the prize for the prettiest child at Mel-
bourne Baby Show, scored 456 votes ; her nearest
competitor only got 171. After all, there must be
some points about a baby. We never could see
any. All babies look the same to us, just as do
all monkeys, Chinamen, and green peas.
# # #
Crime is on the decrease to a remarkable
extent in South Australia. Naracoorte Circuit
Court, held twice a year, has only had one case
since October, 1889—eighteen months ! Sir George
Grey said to the writer that Adelaide was the
first Australian city he had visited where he had
not been accosted by a beggar.

Fitzgibbon is concluding his long reign
as Town Clerk with amusing onslaughts upon
the Early Closing Act. Fitzgibbon is at
least consistent in his generation. He has ever
been the champion of reserves and open places for
the people, and on the principle that shops are
some people’s places, he would keep them all open
until 10 p.m., anyway.
The coming K.’s mama, they state,
In spite of all estrangements
To wipe his scores from off the slate,
At last has made arrangements.
As bright and shining as a star
Our Albert Edward’s face is
For he can play at baccarat
Upon a firmer basis.
He’s not afraid of heavy bets,
He doesn’t fear the draper,
Nor settle incidental debts
With little bits of paper;
The “dicky ” dukes lie couldn’t snub
Regard him now with shyness,
And Hirsch’s shoulder mustn’t rub
The shoulder of His Highness.
E’en Tummy’s tailor breathes again ;
His jeweller grows frisky,
No more he looks with silent pain
At orders that were risky ;
The butcher’s visage doesn't drop
Because he has to trust ’em,
He never crys—“ The other shop
Can ’ave their bloomin’ custom ! ”
A fact is fact, in prose or verse,
And princes (who will doubt it?)
Can do, with money in the purse,
What none can do without it.
An Heir Apparent to the Crown,
Though fairly “ wide ” and knowing,
Gets better served for money down
Than when he leaves it owing.
So, blessings on the Royal Vic.
(Whose praises all the toffs sing),
She doesn’t like us giving tick
To any of her offspring.
She’ll settle Tummy’s debts herself,
To stop the people’s chatter,
And, as they find her royal pelf,
There isn’t much the matter.

Providence, as a rule, maintains a moral
average in humanity by supplying good wives
with bad husbands, and vice versa. Good women
love men for their faults, whilst bad, tricky
charmers lay themselves out for the respectable
Jugginses, the result being children of medium
quality. This proverb has been in the like
Federation, ever since our first parents left Eden,
but it remained for The Bulletin to establish it
on earth. And, by the way, the liking of the bad
man for the other fellow’s wife is always counter-
balanced by the good woman’s scorn for the other
creature’s husband.
* * *
Candidates for the Marlborough Club, to
which Irving was recently admitted, arc either
the nominees of Tummy or must be approved by
him before they are balloted for. The usual cus-
tom is to submit three names to the bald-headed
magnate. If he votes for one of the trio, the
favoured individual is elected as a matter of
course, but if Tummy neither votes nor scowls
ominously, then all three are put up, and the one
who finds most friends among the common or
garden kind of members gets the billet. There is
no “ pilling,” apparently.

A meeting of Sydney parsons has just

odd. For a good rough-and-tumble, a church
meeting takes the cake from the prize-ring.

•* * #
A Melbourne paper, mentioning that the
Mayoress of Oollingwood had been presented
with a silver cradle, went on to say that “the
lady Mayoress responded in suitable terms.”
Gentlemen mayoresses being unknown in these
parts, it is difficult to understand why the recipient
of the gorgeous baby-bunk should be distinguished
by the prefix “lady,” unless it was intended as a
special tribute to her superior breeding. One
might, with more justification, pitch a yarn about
the lady Countess of Jersey desiring her lady
heiress to run away and be good with the lady
governess.
* *
Dr. Strachan, in the Westminster Review,
states that consanguineous marriages are in
all cases nocuous, and must ever prove so in an
increased degree as civilisation proceeds. This is
untrue. In-breeding aggravates existing deformi-
ties, but augments established excellencies. The
subject is a complicated one, but for a rough-and-
ready rule the above is unchallengeable, remarks
“ Junor ” in a Southern paper.

The girl-clerks at Melbourne G.P.O. are
so confused by the recent open-counter innovation
of Postmaster-General Duffy that they can’t sort
out their letters half so fast as formerly. This is
tiardly to be wondered at when you consider the
[earful stare of the remittance-men who cluster
round on the arrival of each British mail.

With the Queen’s'Birthday* at hand, one
wonders if Premier Parkes is going to dine the
Vernon reformatory-ship boys again. An amusing
anecdote has survived of the last feed he gave
them. After swallowing a plate of soup, three
plates of roast beef, six enormous potatoes, half a
plum-pudding, and as much fruit as you could
cram into an ordinary Chinaman’s basket, one
little chap, falling victim to a species of physio-
logical earthquake, addressed his diminutive
stomach in the following well-merited words of
contempt: “ TJmph ! so ye’re beginning to ache,
are ye! I’ll give you something to ache for
directly.” And accordingly passed his plate for
more.
There is a lot of food for thought in the
“Answers to Correspondents” in a fashion-
magazine. Here is a sample, quite typical of
srores of others addressed to more or less damaged
correspondents:—
Do not take potatoes, pastry, milk, cocoa, sugar, nor
coffee ; take plenty of out-door exercise, bathe frequently,
and practise with dumb-bell half-an-hour before break-
fast. It very stout try sea-wrack pills. (2) Take ’s
complexion-pilules. (3) You might try the use of
pumice-stone to remove superflous hair from your face.
(4) The redness is probably caused by indigestion. You
should not drink hot tea if it causes your nose to turn
red ; protect your nose from cold air ; take figs, prunes,
or oranges fasting ; wash your nose with very hot water
and apply the following lotion, &c. A piece of orris-root
held in the mouth will disguise bad breath. (5) lb is
impossible to reduce the size of the hands.
A correspondent like that is almost past repair.
She seems to suffer from nearly everything,
except that her feet aren’t mentioned, and nothing
is said about her ears standing straight out on
each side of her head. Any sane editor would tell
her to escape into a convent.

# * *
The old British nobility with the long-
pedigree —the nobility which wears a haughty
expression and two curly legs that go out at the
knees—is reported to be looking unusually seedy
this year. All the money is in the hands of the
new nobility, which is bow-legged instead of
knock-kneed, and short and fat, and which owns
public-houses, and banks, and property, and trades
in coal and beer, and similar things, and the old
dukes and earls look as if they were wearing their
last shirt. The scene outside Buckingham Palace
at the last royal function drew tears of abject misery
from the Argus’s society female, as she leaned up
against the gate and looked on the decadence of
Hingland. One stupendous flunkey, who drove a
duchess and her two bony daughters, wore a
bouquet made of three camellias and the paper
frill off a boiled ham, and instead of being a proper
flunkey he was a broken-nosed ruffian, evidently
cadged out of a coal-cart. Another peeress of high
degree had two coachmen, but they didn’t match
in point of size, and the correspondent felt that
the country was lost, for they were both smoking
short pipes. A third retainer got down and
hugged a girl from a public-house, while waiting
for his august lord to come out. Most of the
vehicles were one-horse traps of some sort or other,
and were obviously borrowed at 2s. 6d. an hour,
and the flunkeys, as a rule, were badly disguised
individuals from a livery stable or a gutter. There
was no deportment among them, no veneration,
no calves, and their clothes were a horrible misfit,
and they didn’t even know how to assist the
peeresses in or out ot the conveyance, but shoved
them as if they were sacks of beaus. Also, the
horses were a dispirited and broken-kneea lot ot
roarers, and the impression they all left behind
them was that the glory of the empire had de-
parted.

In London Divorce Court, in a case wherein
an Englishwoman applied for a divorce against
her drunken Irish husband, it was decided that
inasmuch as the standard of drunkeness was very
different in Ireland from what it was in England,
what might be called disgraceful insobriety in the
land of the Britisher need not be so considered in
the Emerald Isle. Decree refused accordingly.
Another injustice to Ireland !

It is currently rumoured that a certain
Adelaide paper gets many of its best leading
articles from the local Inebriate Asylum. We
always thought so.
The Goulburn (N.S.W.) Bench has refused
to take the evidence of Dr. Beytes, the Turkish
Consul for N.S.W., on the ground that he de-
clined to remove his skull-cap in court. The
doctor claims that, as he is a Mahommedan, to
take the oath with uncovered head would be
against his conscience and against his caste. He
has complained to the Department of Justice,
which, the facts being as stated, should certainly
see that the Bench apologises for a piece of
very bad manners. We don’t suppose that the
Goulburn Bench is able to force a Hebrew to
swear with uncovered head.

Another Great Win!
AN INVALID FOE 12 YEAES.
Mrs, Fanny Dent, wife of a gentleman employed
in the Lambton Collieries, kindly allows us to
publish the following facts of her interesting case
and wonderful recovery, which we gather from
her correspondence, and which we shall be glad
to allow anyone to peruse who wishes to do so.
Mrs. Dent had been in a state of extreme
debility for twelve years, frequently quite unable
to do any work, the lightest kind causing great
fatigue, with severe pains in the limbs, back and
left side. So severe was the pain that she would
have to sit or lie down frequently. Walking
exercise was very trying and site could only walk
very slowly. She had long seen Clements Tonic
advertised, but like many more people her husband
was prejudiced against “patent medicines,” but
(now we use her own words) “ I knew' some one
who had tried Clements Tonic, and I decided to
get a bottle on the quiet and say nothing about it
if it did me no good. The effect I thought some-
thing wonderful. I felt better after the first dose,
and by the time I had taken half a bottle I was a
different woman. My husband, friends and
neighbours soon noticed the change in me, and
they could not make it out. I had to tell the
truth that it was Clements Tonic that was making
me strong, I could not keep the secret, I was only
too glad to give the credit to Clements Tonic.
After finishing the bottle, work was no trouble
to me, but rather a pleasure, and after doing a
heavy day’s washing I could go for a walk instead
of having to go to bed, as always happened before
taking Clements Tonic. One of my neighbours
said to me last week, ‘ Why Mrs. Dent, you are
doing wonders this week, how is it you are so
strong now ? ’ She knew I had done more that
week than I had been able to do in 12 years before
(she had known me this time, for I am an old
resident of this locality, having lived here for 15
years.) I replied, ‘Yes, I feel a wonder to my-
self, so I’ll tell you the secret, I have been taking
Clements Tonic, and I cannot tell you the benefit
I have derived from it. You know what my
sufferings have been and now I have a genuine
taste of good health.’”
Mrs. Dent wrote us those particulars of her
remarkable cure in March. She wisely, however,
decided to continue the medicine to “clinch the
cure ”as it were. Some time afterwards she wrote
as follows :
Waratah Commonage, N.S.W.
Dear Sir, —It is with great pleasure that I pen
these few lines. I have been as you know a great
sufferer from weakness and debility for years. I
was under several doctors, but they never did any
good- I despaired of ever being better, but as I
stated in my last letter I saw Clements Tonic
advertised. I tried a bottle and its effect was
something wonderful. I felt better almost imme-
diately, and was a new woman after finishing the
first bottle. Before taking Clements Tonic it was
a great trouble even to do a little house-work, but
afterwards my work was a pleasure and my
friends and neighbours were surprised at the
change in me. I cannot say too much in praise of
it, and can only gratefully describe my case and
strongly advise all who suffer from weakness,
backache, pain in the side, ringing noises in the
ears and shortness of breath to take Clements
Tonic. I feel sure it will do them as much good
as it has done me.
Yours Gratefully,
Fanny Dent.
We have great pleasure in thanking this lady
for allowing us to publish the case. We con-
stantly hear of similar ones which people object
to have published through a false delicacy. We
only want to publish them in the interests of
suffering humanity. Surely it is not wrong for
people to acknowledge the genuineness and relia-
bility of an article, when they have proved it to
possess the properties we claim for it,
Consumption and the “Microbe Killer.”
Radam’s Microbe Killer Co. Gentlemen,—l
think it only right to let you know I am getting
on with your great remedy, the Microbe Killer,
and the great benefit that I have received from its
use. Having suffered for some time past from
bad hemorrhage, night sweats, and pneumonia,
the doctors said my lungs were in a very bad
state. I got very much alarmed as I lost a large
quantity of blood, and, like a drowning man
catching at a straw, I tried many remedies, but
with little success. A friend of mine knowing
how ill I had been, advised me to try the Microbe
Killer. I did so, and I am thankful to God, that
I did. After taking the Microbe Killer for about
a fortnight it made me feel very sick, but after-
wards I began to improve. I have now taken
four jars of "the Microbe Killer - two of No. 1, and
two of No. 2—with the result that my appetite has
improved, the night sweats have stopped and I
have had no more hemorrhage, and my health
has greatly improved, and I hope by God’s bles-
sing through the use of your great remedy, that I
shall soon be restored to my usual health and
strength again. I hope that I shall still have
better news to tell you later on. Gentlemen, I
write these few lines for the benefit of the suffer-
ing, believing that if they will give it a fair trial
it will restore them to health. Yours respectfully,
W. Cowdrill, 70 Nelson-street, North Annandale.

St. Patrick’s Day has been defined as the
day on which all the snakes come back to the !
Irishmen.
A South Australian woman lias been very
regular with her Christmas-boxes to her husband.
Her fourth, fifth and sixth child have been born
on each succeeding Christmas eve.
Insist upon having Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Old ‘ 1 Beai'dy Jim,” of Upper Adelong,
after forty years’ hard graft as. a digger, has,been
turned into the shade of the Liverpool (N.S.W.)
Benevolent Asylum to end his days. He is eighty
years’old.
Wolfe’s Schnapps Will make you a new man.
A peculiarity of N.S.W. District Court
Judge Backhouse, is that he lias the habit of look-
ing intently for a few moments at each prisoner
as he or she is placed in the dock, and seldom
or never glances again in the direction of the
accused till the end of the trial, though it may
last for days.

Notice to booksellers and
OTHERS.
Booksellers, Librarians, Collectors, and others,
are hereby cautioned against purchasing four
volumes of the Hobart Town Gazette, for the
years 1821, 1824, 1825, and 1832, bound in half-
calf, cloth sides, the same having been stolen.
Each volume, in addition to the official index,
contains a manuscript abstract of contents. One
volume bears the autograph “ Story, Richmond,”
on first page, and all have, inside cover, the book
label of “H. T. Dwight, Bookseller, Melbourne,”
that bookseller having apparently got them
through the late John Pascoe Fawkner, as Mr.
Fawkner’s handwriting can be identified on
margins. Present holder will not be disturbed in
possession of the volumes if obtained by him oi-
lier in good faith, upon indicating the channel
through which they were obtained. Two pounds
reward will be paid for any information (coming
from any other person than such holder) that will
lead to the discovery of these volumes. Address :
“ Price Waning,” Bulletin Office, Sydney.

A Victorian non-unionist has written to
the Government suggesting that executioner Jones
should be sacked as he—-the writer —is willing to
do the strangulation and flogging at a reduced
rate. This is the ancient trouble—we are ruined
by cheap Welsh hanging and unprincipled blackleg
execution, and the same unhappy tendency to
sacrifice efficiency to mere economy, which has
already ruined so many promising industries,
threatens to invade this one also. Hitherto it has
been the recognised axiom that the thoroughness
and open-hearted lavishness of the flogging and
scragging business was more to be considered than
questions of mere finance, and when the Anglo-
Saxon race begins to go back on this great rule the
empire will be on the downward track. It is to
be hoped that the Victorian Government recog-
nises this fact, and will repulse the cheap applicant
with scorn. To do otherwise would be to east a
slur on the present energetic and painstaking
hangman, and then he would probably begin to
lose interest in his work, and wouldn’t fix the
knot under the left ear with his old enthusiasm.
Even a hangman has feelings, and when that
conscientious officer comes home at night, and
hangs up the noose in the boudoir, and stows
away the cat among the family archives, he should
be able to reflect that his country is not ungrateful
for his efforts. And when he is off duty, in a
manner of speaking, and resting peacefully on the
lawn among the overshadowing trees, while his
son and heir is playfully hanging the infant on
the knob of the front door, and his blue-eyed
daughter is passing sentence of death on the
kitten, or ordering the nurse to receive fifty lashes
and then be imprisoned with hard labour for the
term of her natural life, it is ungenerous that his
comfort should be marred by the fear that a cheap
scab executioner may presently jump his billet.
And when he dies in the fulness of years he
should be able to hand an unblemished gallows
down to his posterity, and if he be compelled, by
unprincipled competition, to hang below tariff
rates it is obvious he can’t do that, and must
perish miserably without even his own self-respect
to sustain him in his last hours. The writer of
this paragraph tried to end with a poem, but as
the nearest rhyme he could get for “hangman”
was “bang him” it has been held over for
farther consideration.

Brown’s Buck.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Billy Brown is a Bogalong digger,
He has “followed the game up ” for years,
But his pile hasn’t grown any bigger,
And his luck would just move you to tears—
He has bottomed more duffers than any
Who have tackled the shovel and pick,
Since twenty-five years ago, when he
Went prospecting with Pigeon-toed Mick.
He has worked till he couldn’t be colder,
Belly-deep in the wettest of sludge,
But to find his way stopped by a boulder
That a dynamite-bomb wouldn’t budge;
Through both hard and soft sinking lie’s driven,
He has belted with hammer and gad ;
He has blasted, and sweated, and striven,
But his luck’s been invariably bad.
So it seemed, t’other day, quite surprising
That old Brown had, at last, a “ good thing,”
When he sold out a claim, realising
Just a tenner—’twas all it would bring.
In that claim he’d been sinking and sloping,
There were drives going through it all ways—
Till old Brown, in despair, gave up hoping
To be able to say—“ My claim pays.”
’Twas a claim a man’s feelings to harrow,
For the ground was the hardest about,
And the wash was but poor stuff and narrow—
She had “pinched,” and was near “duffered
out.”
So Brown jumped at the chance of resigning
All his right to the “ Enterprise ” block
To three “mugs ” who knew nothing of mining—
Men who couldn’t tell felspar from rock !
They had come from “the old country,” thinking
They would pile up a fortune with ease;
They knew less than a child about sinking
(They had not worn the skin off their knees !).
On the subjects of “ground,” “lodes,” “forma-
tion,”
They were just about equally wise :
Which accounts for this fool combination
Giving “ten ” for the old “Enterprise.”
And old Bill softly murmured—“ They’re had
‘ some,’
By-and-bye they will cuss it and me !”
And he straightway embarked on a gladsome
And a wildly hilarious spree.
And he bragged, till to listen was painful,
Of his deal he unceasingly jawed ;
And his words were sarcastic, disdainful.
“ Wish ’em joy of the bloominold fraud ! ”
But an end came at last, to the gladness
Of the man who had sold out his claim,
From the pub. he was fired out in sadness,
With not even a cent to his name.
He had “boozed up ” the whole of his ready,
He was sick from the rums he had quaffed,
As he zigzagged, with footsteps unsteady,
To the sold-out old “Enterprise ” shaft.
At the claim they were busily working,
And a whip horse was hauling out stuff
Just as fast as he could do—no shirking—
And at that wasn’t half quick enough !
For the men on the forty-foot level,
Had just only to shovel it in,
And they cussed that slow horse like the devil—
More than half of the stuff ivas pure tin !
’Twas explained to old Brown :—“We intended
To go on where you’d driven before;
So began at the place where you ended,
And drove her ’bout thirteen feet morej
And she opened out wider an’ wider,
Just as nice as could possibly be !
And there’s ever so much more inside her,
Would you like to go under and see ? ”
Thus those mugs made the greatest of “ splashes ”
There’s been nothing to equal it since—
And they now cut the biggest of dashes
(It is said one’s on terms with a Prince !)
If you search the swell newspapers’ pages,
You will see them named line after line;
And old Brown is now ivorking for 'wages,
T n the world-famous Enterprise mine!
A. CHEE.
