Personal Items.
Melba’s little son is six years old, “and a
great comfort to her.”
“ Federation is as dead as Julius Ceesar.
And so is poor old Jack Robertson.
Parkes has not yet achieved greatness.
London Punch has never published his portrait.
Bradlaugh described the Review of
Reviews as “ hysterics tempered by huckster-
ing.”
The estimable Fffienezer Gourlay, now or
Europe, doesn’t seem to have left much property
behind for his Melbourne creditors to seize.
A brother of the Marquis of Queensberry
has committed suicide. Why didn’t he fight
himself to a finish under his noble relative’s rules?
Sir John Robertson is the third ex-Colonial
Secretary of N.S.W. found dead in bed. Michael
Fitzpatrick and William Forster were the other
two.
Prince Napoleon’s ghost walks Melbourne
streets, in the person of F. G. Smith, National
Bank manager, and president of the Melbourne
Cricket Club.
Stead, in his Review, gushed over Grant
Allen, and Allen, in a Melbourne letter, calls him
“one of the three greatest men in England.”
“Scratch,” &o.
The tallest public men about Melbourne
are Joe Symes, Panton and M‘Kean. Though
these people have all very long bodies, two of
them have very short minds.
Thus Andrew Lang : “In Stevenson the
striking quality is the buoyancy, the survival of
the child in him ; in Kipling, the swift and cer-
tain vision, his certainty in effects.”
Frank Stuart, by joining the Government
in a “no portfolio” capacity, became entitled to
a free-pass on the Victorian railways for life.
That's worth getting, in these hard times.
Acting-Governor Judge Boucaut (S.A.)
came in for £15,000 as son-in-law of the late Alex.
M'Culloch. The Judge has relinquished his idea
of re-entering politics, and will probably go in for
yachting.
They say that when Sir William Gordon"
Gumming brings his baccarat-scandal to court, h e
will be non-suited, on a technical point, before
Tummy and his friends can be put into the
witness-box.
Archbishop Carr (R.C.), of Melbourne,
quite abjures the dignity affected by Archbishop
Goold, billing himself simply as “T. J. Carr
D.D.,” and signing himself “ T. J. Carr,” instead
of “T. Melbourne.”
The late Charles Bradlaugh was once in
communication with R. S. Smythe for an Austra-
lian lecturing tour. If the Much-Travelled could
have brought him to terms, the great Liberator
might have been alive to-day.
Mooshouse has another chance for the
York Archbishopric, through Magee’s death.
Fancy Moorhouse’s excitement when he heard
the shocking news—why are such temptations
placed before poor human frailty ?
Mr, F. W. Ward, late editor of Sydney
Daily Telegraph and formerly editor of Sydney
Echo, goes to England to act as cable-agent for a
big Australian press combination, taking with him
the cordial goodwill of all pressmen.
Sir George Grey ; “ Broken Hill is the
eighth wonder of the world ’’—this is dangerously
near the style in which Lady Bowen used to kiss
the blarney-stone in New Zealand —“Auckland
reminds me of my beautiful Isles of Greece.”
Turning up London Athenaeum for 1862,
one drops on the first notice of Kendall’s poems.
He sent them in MSS., being then only 20, and a
generous three-column notice was given. Very
different to the Quarterly Review on Keats.
Phil. May (who, by the way, has of late
been very ill) is to be art-editor of the new London
comic, Big Ben, it is said. It is to be in style
something between N.Y. Puck and Paris Figaro.
Four pages in colours, eight in black and white.
“Trickettand Hanlan,” says Dr. Benjamin
Ward Richardson, “are equally emphatic about
the injury inflicted by the use of narcotics and
stimulants.” The point of the moral lies in the
application thereof ; Trickett and Hanlan both
kept pubs.
“On the 21»t ulfc. Sir Henry Parkes
received a letter, signed ‘ Harry,’ intimating that
he would be killed on May 17. The identity of
‘Harry ’ is suspected, and a warrant for his arrest
has been issued.” Old Harry, probably. The
Kiama Ghost again.
A certain member of the Griffith Ministry,
recently asked by a party of Sunday-school
teachers if he did not think that a man like Par-
nell should be kicked out of Parliament, replied
with more than Ministerial honesty; “No, my
friends. Consider where I should be then.”
Moltke’s romance, romance of the steel-
faced man. His sister married Widower Burt
(English) with a daughter 16, who fell in love with
Moltke’s letters, though he was 40 ; they had 25
years of happy married worship. Her memory
Moltke carried to his tomb, 23 years a widower.
If the Queen’s Proctor really interferes in
the matter of O’Shea’s rule nisi, with a view to
the rehearing of the Parnell case and the examina-
tion of Parnell and Mrs. O’Shea, his action, you
may be sure, will have the sinister object of
endeavouring to entrap Parnell on a charge of
perjury. That is the game.
The S.M. Herald says of the late Lord
Granville, that “he, the Prince of Wales, and
Earl Lytton are said to surpass everybody else in
making a speech in French. This reminds one of
a Bismarckism; “The Englishman who speaks
French is always a rascal.” Who, during his walk
through life, has not found this to be true ?
Mr. Tatham, a self-educated artisan of
Balmain, Sydney, claims to have cheaply pro-
duced a eoal-and-air-gas with an illuminating
power of eight-and-a-half times that of ordinary
gas. He has received so much encouragement
from the world’s highest scientific authorities that
he proceeds to Europe to deal with his patent.
Parson Scott, of Brisbane, says he hears
the voice of the Lord in the call to Pitt-street
Church, Sydney, where he is to get £3OO a year
more than he was getting in Queensland. They
all do it. Brisbane didn’t suit the pastor’s
health, but The Bulletin predicts that the atr of
Sydney will agree with him uncommonly well.
Sir George Grey may not sit in the Maori-
land Parliament after all. He has a great desire
to revisit England, and, moreover, the climate of
Wellington would, it is much feared, not improve
the lung-affection and bronchitis from which he
suffers. He will decide the matter on his return
to Maoriland, the W. Australian trip having been
abandoned.
It has been said that “Plon-Plon” is a
corruption of “ Craint-Plomb,” or “fear-bullet,”
but Archibald Forbes asserts that it was a boyish
nickname of Prince Napoleon’s, derived from
plonger, to pitch, to dip. “ Plon-Plon,” he says,
“ I take to signify a very waddling waddler; and
that is just what in the matter of gait Jerome was
through life.”
The present constituents of Duncan
Gillies are railing against him, the old Rodney
district has slipped from his clutch for ever, and
the next Victorian elections will probably leave
Duncan without a seat whereon to rest his
pompous fat. An idol humbled in the dust of un-
belief, who hasn’t so much as a wife to whisk the
feather brush over him once a week.
Among the moderate drinkers of modern
times may be reckoned J. E. Boehm, R. A.
Proctor, Mark Twain, W. E. Gladstone, E. A.
Freeman, J. A. Froude, Matthew Arnold, Charles
Darwin, Wilkie Collins, and our modest selves.
All profess or professed temperance both in wine
and smoking. Nearly all the great Frenchmen,
Germans and Russians are non-teetotallers.
Edmund Yates, for printing in the World
a paragraph imputing unchastity to a fashionable
lady, was sent to gaol for four months. Before
the hearing “ Wales ” was asked to intercede and
square the matter, but refused. Yates then evi-
dently declared a vendetta against Wales, and has
made things very hot for him —firstly in re the
baccarat scandal, and secondly by declaring
Wales’ insolvency.
Our favourite Wesleyanic literature just
now teems with John Wesley. His love was
Grace Murray, with whom he starred innocently
round Ireland. But Brother Charles intrigued to
break off the match, because she was a mere ser-
vant, and she was persuaded to immolate herself
on the altar of Brother Bennett, after which John
fell back on a Xantippe, hair-shirt wife, Widow
Fizelle—or Yazille.
London Truth says that George Eliot’s
hasty second marriage, inexplicable even to her
dearest friends, is now accounted for by the state-
ment that “circumstances came to her knowledge
a few weeks after the death of Lewes which froze
up her enthusiasm for his memory.” The lone
widow generally finds an excuse for marrying
again. The absent are always wrong, and there
are none so absent as the men in wooden over-
coats.
Beat this for impudence and cruelty. Old
Tom Keene, octogenarian, who rode in the
Balaclava Charge, applied to Prahran, Melbourne,
Bench last week for relief, having had to pawn his
medals. He wears a plate on his head where he
got a sabre-cut; he was also sabred on the
shoulder and shot in the leg. The chairman of
the bench said it was disgraceful for Her
Majesty’s soldier to pawn his medals, and packed
him off.
It is not generally known that the late
John Brown used to cuss and swear “ something
’orrid ” at the Queen’s favourite moke, when that
overburdened beast objected to carrying her round
the Windsor Castle grounds. At such times
Y.R. would look reproachfully upon the faithful
gillie and say, “Hush, John, I’m surprised at
you!” And John would reply, “Hech, your
Mojesty, it’s this domned dunkey ye ought to be
surprised at, I’m thinkin’.”
Tough old Burtt, aged 83, was to the fore
at Melbourne Eight-hour Demonstration. Not-
withstanding that he is a Munroic teetotaller, he
used to be absolutely unintelligible in the Vic-
torian Parliament, while men like Fraser and
Grant could speak straight sometimes. In 1866
Burtt proposed the Eight-hours resolution at
Queen’s Theatre, but it is disputed whether Gal-
loway or Stephens was the founder, though Gallo-
way got the monument.
Here are the facts of an affair which has
been garbled all sorts of ways. In 1863, when
Palmerston was an octogenarian, one O’Kane
proceeded against him, as co-respondent, in the
divorce court. Pam. being then Premier of England,
damages laid'£2o,ooo. Mrs. O’Kane pleaded that
she was never married, which upset the case, and
it was withdrawn. Palmerston did not plead, but
claimed further affidavits.
Little John Finnamore is a not un-remark-
able man, to be seen in Melbourne. Long ago his
tragedy of “ Carpio ” was warmly praised by
leading London periodicals, but the atmosphere
does not appear to have been favourable to his
poetic development. A departed genius named
R. R. Campbell put in a few touches to “Carpio.”
Campbell was drowned in a terrible Sunday boat-
accident, while a festive party was returning from
John Everard’s Schnapper Point.
Honourable old Macredie, Rip Van
Winkle of Melbourne insurance world, has taken
a safe A 1 risk for the other. He was concerned
in insurances on the Blockade Runners, at Ameri-
can Civil War time, and such business relations
partly accounted for the enthusiastic welcome
accorded to Waddell, and the sharky Shenandoah,
by the Melbourne Club. And handsomely did
England pay through the nose, for the Shenan-
doah’s Melbourne docking, in defiance of Consul
Sam Lord's protests.
When Sir George Grey was Governor of
South Africa, the Duke of Edinburgh, then
16 years old, was sent out to him for “ colonial
experience.” Sir George took Alfred on a hunting
expedition and they got lost, but ultimately struck
a Dutch settler’s hut. The settler’s wife had
nothing but some flour and some grease from the
fat tail of the South African sheep. Sir George
refused this with disgust, but Alfred, who had a
boy’s appetite, rolled the mixture up into a pan-
cake and swallowed it with zest.
The poet Holdsworth, when cashier in the
N.S.W. Treasury, once appeared as witness in an
embezzlement-case. Upon being asked by Frank
Rogers, Q.C., for the defence, if he remembered
certain entries in the cash-book, “Joe” appealed
to the Judge with “ Your Honor, it would take a
Simonides to remember each item.” “Eh, who,
what’s that?” said Rogers. “Simonides,” rolled
out P.J. in his most sonorous tones, “Si-monides,
the great—-Greek—au-thority upon mne-monics.”
“ Ah, ah,” said Rog.ers, “ I knew him well.”
For larcenous plagiarism nothing ever beat
Rev. Dr. Jobson’s steal, in his “ Australian
Travels,” from Frank Fowler’s “Lights and
Shadows of Southern Life.” Poor Frank, like
the “ Vagabond,” had crabbed the Wesleyans
somehow, and so was curious over Jobson, but
stood aghast at the wholesale stealing (the worst
bit being over a shark-story) which he fully ex-
posed by parallel columns in London Athenaeum.
Said Frank : “A whale appropriated a reverend
gentleman, and now one appropriates my shark.”
That dear, dirty, old Shah, over whom
England has twice gushed, is said to have lured
one of his escaped Ministers back to Persia by
false promises, and then to have ordered the poor
wretch to be sawn in halves. The late Persian
ambassador in London, fearful of a death with
“boiling oil or molten lead in it ” if he returned
to Ins beloved Sovereign, has retired into private
life in the Piccadilly district. Moreover, he has
written to the Times, explaining what a mean,
base, bloodthirsty beast the bejewelled Padishah
really is.
Litterateur James Smith, of Melbourne,
like all literary men, and actors not a few, thinks
his powers are quite misapplied, for while the
public gulp him on the drama, art, and aesthetic
cackle, he wants to dose them with the kind of stuff
housed to serve up in the Maryborough (Vic.)
Advertiser. There were vivid proclamations of
how near Doomsday is, with discursions on the
fearful increase of crime, wars, and natural con-
vulsions. If anyone wants an excursus about the
Blanco Encelada’s sanguinary bombardment of
Pisagua, followed by her blow-up by the torpedo,/
(James is on. /
The U.S. Government is still paying
pensions to twenty-three widows whose husbands
fought in the revolutionary war, despite the fact
that the American revolution just 109
years ago. The oldest of these females is 98 and
the youngest only 72, consequently there is a
difficulty about crowding the latter person in.
It is supposed, however, that her husband started
fighting along with Washington when he was 14,
and then lived peacefully as a bachelor till the age
of 73, at which period he married a girl of 16 who
has now grown into the widow aforementioned.
This is a tight fit, but the widow got in and
collects her pension regularly.

4th May. W. G. SPENCE.
Brisbane owes many prominent figures in
the present labour struggle to the incubating influ-
ence of the compositors’ frame. Buzacott, pro-
prietor of the Courier;; Moreley, editor of the
Observer ; Lane, editor of the Worker ; Hinch-
cliffe, secretary of the Labour Federation; Walker,
envoy extraordinary to the Argentine Republic,
and Crawford, all at one time earned their liveli-
hood as compositors. J. D. Fitzgerald was also a
compositor, and is now a proof-reader on the
Australian Star. Henry George began life as a
“ comp.,” and Mark Twain once set type on the
Golden Age, a San Francisco weekly of which
Walter Adams, son of the ex-Surveyor General of
N.S.W., was subsequently editor and part-pro-
prietor.
Reseigh Martin writes that the powerful
influence of the Synagogue has been exercised
from time to time in favour of the release of the
murderer, Bertrand, who has now been 26 years
in gaol, and not so long ago, at the close of Mr.
William Clarke’s term of office as N.S. W. Minister
of Justice, these efforts were so close to fruition
that he was dressed in a tailor-made suit of
clothes, his belongings were packed, and the date
of his departure fixed, when the veto of Sir
Alfred Stephen (the Judge who presided at his
trial, and certain of whose family, by the way, are
popularly believed to have been mentioned in
Bertrand’s diary) interposed, and the wretched
man was ordered back into convict’s uniform, and
told in effect to wait till the clouds roll by.
Martin also says that Wadham Wyndham, who,
while religiously mad, slew his wife and two of his
children, is now sane and at Darlinghurst.

Bound the Shops and Shows.
Listen ladies ! “ Silver threads among
the gold.” It sounds very poetical, of course,
but was there ever a woman who didn’t prefer her
own golden or black tresses minus these silvern
streaks of time? Certainly not, therefore why
not call upon Mr. G. Von Brakkel, 32 King-
street, and procure some of his vegetable hair-
restorer? It is a wash of his own invention, and
although it restores gray hair to its original colour,
it has no evil effects like most washes. The most
becoming fringes to match any hair are always on
hand at this establishment, and they are made so
beautifully and look so natural that they can be
worn without the slightest fear of detection.
* # #
It is at Messrs. Cecil Hordern and Co.’s,
486, 488, and 490 George-street, that those splen-
did tailor-made ladies’ Berlin ulsters, cloaks, and
mantles are to be seen. These articles are genuine
bargains, costing little over 305., and are made
in the latest styles and finished off in a manner
which is usually only seen in the most expensive
cloaks. Now is the time to invest, as the cold
winds are beginning to be felt and bargains like
these are not to be met with every day. _ Messrs.
Cecil Hordern are also showing some choice opera
cloaks, and plush jackets with Astrakhan trim-
ming, from 15?. upwards.

Dining With. Death.
The death of the United States Secretary of the
Treasury, Windom, was sudden and tragic.
Many of our public men have been stricken by the
hand of the destroyer at the post of duty; in
Windom's case the blow fell during a brief interval
given to social recreation—he was dining with
death. It is hard to make the people believe it,
but the man who accepts a post as chief executive
of any of the great departments of the nation, and
does his duty, is taking his life in his hands.
Many of these officials live through the ordeal,
but few come out of office in good health.
Folger, Manning, and now Windom, three
victims in less than a decade of devotion to the
exacting duties and immense responsibilities of the
proper care of the national treasury. What killed
them? Not solely over-work ; many men perform
as many hours of labour every day as did either
of these men, without impairing their vital
energies. It is the immense mental strain upon
men filling great executive positions that
gradually, but surely, saps their physical strength.
They feel themselves gradually giving way, but
they cannot pull up. There is no cessation of the
drain upon their physical powers, and finally
some fateful link in the chain snaps and the end
is reached. They might have been saved ?
Doubtless ; many a man has been, by a simple
process. When a man begins to feel the fatigue
and weakness of physical, combined with mental,
over-work, he should see that the great purifying
organs of the system, the liver and kidneys, are in
order for the work they have to perform—re-
moving all impurities from the system, so that
nature may not be hindered, but aided, in its
labour of restoring health and strength. The
notable victims of over-work we have named were
strong, hearty, healthy men, naturally. If they
had kept liver and kidneys in healthy working
order by the use of Warner’s safe cure, which
never fails, the great strain upon their natural
powers might have been much longer borne, at
least, if not entirely neutralised. Alfred Cutler,
of Surry Hills, Victoria, writes us:—“Having
been subjected to the trying climate of the North
of Queensland for a number of years, my health
gave way, and I suffered from liver complaint of an
aggravated nature. I tried a number of medicines,
but ray health remained impaired, and soon after-
wards I was taken with kidney disease. I now
procured Warner’s safe cure, and have no doubt
that had I hesitated I would have fallen a prey to
the dread Bright’s disease. With the use of a
number of bottles of safe cure, my health was
restored, and I was completely cured.”

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, May 13,1891.
Uv Dear Mooramnda,—
“ Society,” tapering down from Lord and Lady Charles
Scott to a curly white poodle in a plush jacket edged with
brass bells (certainly in the topmost wave of doggy style)
turned out in great force at the Rushcutters’ Bay Oval, on
9th inst., when the Zingari team (Lord Jersey, Lord
Ancrum, Goschen, A. Knox, S. E. Laidley, C. H. Hodgson,
“ Sid ” Cape, and one or two others) met the Fleet, headed
by Admiral Scott, and some sensational cricket ensued.
The leading feature of the field was Lord Jersey (wasn’t
it rather odd that His Ex. should play cricket with Sir
John Robertson’s exit ? The Caringtons put off a party
on the occasion of Hon. James White’s decease) who
looked limper still in a checkless suit of white flannej
braided in navy-blue, topped by a forlorn felt hat. His
score was three runs,-and his “fielding” was most in-
teresting, for every time the ball was struck His Excel-
lency would drop into a position of exaggerated watchful-
ness, no matter even if the leathern sphere were whirling
in quite an opposite direction. Some of the players, who
ostentatiously displayed their coats of arms on portions
of their wearing-apparel, gave quite an “ old family ” air
to the scene, though one youth who wore his crest on his
sash, handkerchief and cap, did not soothe, for the family
sign occurred too often and got to look like the brand of
his particular asylum. Afternoon-tea was served in a
striped tent, and as the invitations had not been issued
broadcast late comers were not confronted by tea-leaves
and emptied dishes.
As for the drapings on view, Lady Soott (who will stay
with her people in Melbourne until Admiralty House is
fixed up) wore a black gown embroidered at the foot in
dull crimson and gold, covered by a crimson cloak braided
in black and pinked at the edges; black hat trimmed
with black velvet, ostrich tips, and touches of crimson
velyet; Miss Nellie Hill, dark-blue with relSvements of
blue spot with white, black toreador hat trimmed with
black velvet; Miss M. Cox, fawn tweed and guipure with
scarf of pink chiffon, fawn felt hat trimmed with fawn
ribbons ; Miss Marks, dark slate-blue with vest of mush-
room SuMe, toreador hat trimmed with blue velvet.
Another nice frock was in dark-green cloth braided in
black and finished off in otter fur, wide green velvet hat
and feathers. But what can be said of the girl who
sported a glaring scarlet and yellow tartan aggravated by
a pair of old-fashioned velvet sleeves? Or of another
Vivid lady in light-fawn tweed with a tomato stripe over
a full Garibaldi skirt of red silk—and tan boots ?
The second day of the Lawn Tennis tournament on
Sydney Association Ground, was not largely attended, bub
all the women present were nicely dressed, and there was
a fair peppering of men. Lady Jersey was ushered in ab
8.30 to the strains of the National Anathema, wearing a-
dark-blue hard-felt hat, a size too loose, and buttercup
boots, so the small assemblage was vice-regal at any rate.
There doesn’t seem to be half the public interest taken in
tennis this year as was shown at the ’9O matches—perhaps;
because Victoria, abetted by English champion Eaves, is
making hay of the local cracks, and everybody doesn’t
like to fume on an Austrian chair watching the defeat of
one’s own tribe. Eaves (who plays a galvanic game) is a
short, muscular youth with a boarding-school-girl’s dream
on his upper lip and “ yaller ’’ boots. But he really
shouldn’t play as he does without a cap, not because of
the risk of un coup de soleil (you see I am polishing up-
for the Bernhardt), but because he is just a little bib
billiard-bally on the top. The ground was in perfect con-
dition, just as smooth as cloth, and the band and the
afternoon-tea were satisfactory—but why did they choose'
a boy-let with a contracting patch on his pants, the size
of the map of Asia, to go about head-first after the;
balls ?
Whilst the Jerseys were doing (Maitland, the leading-
masher of that township was seated at the inevitable;
spread, at the right-hand of His Ex., who was smiling a.
gracious smile which lured the unwary masher on to his-
downfall. Stretching out a very muddy boot for the-
Governor’s inspection, he chattily remarked: “ There’s a
state for a boot to be in after a ten-mile ride ! Could your
Excellency tell me what sort of a riding-boot is fashion-
able in England just now ?” But with a forbidding glare;
in his nearest eye, Lord J, answered, shortly, that he
“took no interest in boots,” and turned one sloping
shoulder on his now wilting tormentor.
Sydney Government House garden-party on 6th inst.
for which 1500 cards had been issued, was further-
augmented by a crowd of uninvited people who swarmed-
in under the delusion that “it” was a reception, and;
were politely allowed to infest the premises by Lord and;
Lady J., who received their lawful and unlawful visitants;
in the garden before the entrance. Lady Jersey—who-
always dresses better at home than abroad—wore a French-
grey reception toilet, made en basque, bonnet of gold'
straw, white chiffon, and front of pink roses With strings •
Lord Jersey, in an amethyst tie, of course; the little
Jerseys in pink-striped pongee, Leghorn hats and
feathers. The arrangements were the same as for an At-
Home—a band of music and unlimited lemonade. In
fact, it was a reception on a more “ select” basis than -
when all sorts of people are allowed to hurtle in and no-
questions are asked. If Government House is “society’’
or its Australian prototype), and the Town Hall “civic,”
(he lady in the crinoline and lasting boots ought not to
rage if she is obliged to keep her blue-and-purple bonnet-
in lavender Until the Mayoress is at home.
The gowning at the garden-party was resurrection as;
usual, and as the day was warm, diverse: for some were-
cold and some were hot, So some had furs and some had
not; and some were rouged and some were pale (but-
all were on the “cakes and ale.”) Glaring hues pre-
dominated—one woman in the new crimson with a spiky
hat fixed With gold almost giving one the eye-aohe when
(he iud caught her.
tt must be very dreadful to be st vice-regal person
addressing the average humble “loyal” individual, One
of the tribe who are afraid to say their Soul’s their own
if a coronet happens to be looking round the corner. I
was interested in observing the detneanour of one of these
the other day as she was “taken notice of” by Lady
Jersey. Really the woman looked as if she were a convicted
criminal on eight separate charges, for she hung hfer head
and blushed like a toihato, and hardly could put two
words together from sheer fright. She was so altogether
flabbergasted by the unexpected attention that, if I had
been Lady J., I should have been strongly tempted to say
“ Pray don’t be so afraid--I am not going to bite you.”
In the teeth of the winter, with its attendant blue-cold
noses and wrinkled brows, lovely woman rushes for pearl-
cream and rose de beaute, which get a slight reprieve
during the melting summer, when rouge and Whitewash
are too apt to mingle in one blotchy patch. Some of the
frescoes that I have remarked at recent social gatherings
are Very convincingly arranged, and one artist especially
had worked the carmine so carefully into her jawbone
(they mostly put it on in a round patch of unsoftened
dye, like a pantaloon does) that I really felt I needed the
advice of a male friend who confided to me his secret cf
how to discover whether a woman is naturally blushing or
not. His plan is (he is a bold and graceless boy) “to
touch their faces somehow”—as he puts it, for of course
he doesn’t tell me whether he does it with his moustache
or not. If her cheek is warm en suite to her cherry ripe.
Bess, he concludes she is genuine ; but if she is cold and
pink—like cochineal blanc-mange—then he views her
mantling hue only as an alleged improvement bought at
the chemist’s at so much a bottle.
Mrs. Bland Holt re-appeared at Sydney Royal on 9th
inst., in “The Bells of Haslemere” —an emotional drama
in which Walter Howe, as the good young man, exercises
his flighty back-stop hand-shake, as usual, and some o*
the dancing supes clutch at their clothing as desperately
as if they were holding it on. In the first act, Mrs. Holt
(who received a charming bouquet of ’mums, ferns, roses >
etc.) was girlish in a soft white gown, wide hat trimmed
with scarlet poppies ; Kate Bishop wearing grey with
yoke and cuffs of white embroidery, grey hat with touches
of crimson ; Blanche Lewis in pale-pink draperies and a
very pretty pinky hat. In act three, Maggie Knight
reappears, wearing a soft white muslin with full sleeves,
and sash, tied frontally, of spring-green. In act four, Mrs.
Holt wears a well-fitting gown in large brown and crimson
checks trimmed on a bodice with bands of black velvet 1
Kate Bishop, leaf-brown skirt and long brown velvet
jacket over a pale-blue vest; artistic brown hat with
touches of blue.
An all-wool d.c. assembled and gathered together at
Her Majesty’s Majeroni matinee on 6th instant, with
Lord and Lady Jersey and all the little Jerseys in the
red-satin enclosure dedicated to distinguished itinerances*
and a bevy of beauty and fashion in stalls and circle"
The frocking wasn’t altogether an unmixed blessing, for
one lady was frantically arrayed in orange-colour
surmounted by a hat in red and kingfisher-blue, and
another assault on chromatics was a sort of cabbage-
and-gravy mottle, relieved with gold braid and a hat
like a riot in a prism. Lady Jersey (who will scarcely be
the cause of a rush of the bong-tong to her couturifere’s)
wore a pinky heliotrope silk trimmed with bands of
velvet of the same shade, white bonnet with front of pink
roses and white strings, white fall and cream lace
parasol; the little Jersey girls alike, in cream frocks, flat,
leghorn hats and feathers. Other people present were
Mrs. Harry M'Quade (who sold 150 tickets) in a box,
with a white gown and black hat on ; Miss Nellie Hill in
dark-green and tartan ; Sirs. Victor Cohen in fawn cloth
and browm silk ; Mrs. J. H. Carruthers in black crepe
cloth ; Mrs. Bland Holt (who brought the Misses Coppin,
from Melbourne) in biscuit cloth and dragon-blue silk
trimmings ; Miss M. Windeyer in dark grey-blue silk and
flat white hat and feathers. The matinee realised £450
“ in round numbers.”
I want to find fault with the Engleesh as she is spoke
just now on the Sydney stage generally. One gang of
presumably educated mummers invariably speak Of
England as “ Angland the other day I heard a remark
that the “song was sang.” Piano is “piahno,” and
aitches are scattered broadcast. It really can’t be good
for one’s ear to have to listen to no matter how pretty a
woman remarking ecstatically, " 0 ow appy I am !”—can
It? And a thing that makes me doubt the book-learning
•of average songsters is that when a breach of Lindley
Murray occurs in a lilt they do not correct it, but blindly
warble on according to the printer.

Respectable “girl ” of 80 is depressing ; I wonder when
a, single woman ceases to regard herself as a “Kittenish
creature standing with reluctant feet,” &c. ? One of the
greatest drawbacks to spinsterhood is that perpetual
friskiness which leads to tanned boots and tight corsets
at 50. A matron of similar age is content to bonnet and
cloak herself, but the unclaimed blessings pin a floral
hat on their skewbald locks and accentuate their bony
frames in skin-fit clothes to the verge of the tomb«
instead of recognising the years they have passed by.
At the second performance of “Masks and Faces ” at the
Garrick, there was a very full-fig circle. A Consul’s wife,
in black, wore a magnificent ruby necklace ; Mrs. B. R-
Wise was artistic in soft brown velvet with Vandyke
•collar, and a gown of pink chiffon, very daintily cut, was
nice enough for anything. A Lord was there, helping him-
self in a very unconventional manner to flowers out of the
■corsage-bouquet of a fair damsel; and two girls, in blue
plush cloaks with bordeaux of imitation diamonds, would
have looked more convincing behind the floats. As “ Peg
Woffington” Miss Achurch wears in the first Act yellow
satin skirt and sleeves with an over-gown of black, edged
with silver lace and bound on the bodice with orange
ribbons, black hat with mass of shaded orange feathers,
•ebony stick with “ diamond” head. In Act 2, an orange-
coloured silk underdress, with the train of blue chintz
pattern covered in spangles. In Act 3, blue brocade petti,
•coat with Dolly Varden polonaise in cream figured satin )
with ruches of cream and blue. Miss Bronton’s one gown
■of mushroom woollen (aren’t the pink chiffon frills

•bodice.

A deputation from the Sydney Ladies’ Club bearded
■Curator Moore in his den recently, and induced him to
allow penny chairs on the Botanic Gardens iawn during
Band Wednesdays. Mr. Moore would not agree to the
other proposition—that the ,Domain drive be rendered
circular by joining Macquarie-street at the Quay end. It
wasn’t exactly feasible. Did the carriage mean to go
bumping down the steps at the end of the Appian Way?

his women-patients to the practice of wearing chrysan-
themums close to the face. Not,too, much talking,
Doctor?

You ask how industrialism is to be carried on when there

non-unionist as the enemy of the human race

Stage Versicles.
The Girl Who’s Been “Betrayed.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
We want a melodrama now —
A thrilling four-act show—
Devoid of her who beats her brow
And utters cries of woe :
Aweary are we of the “ tart ”
(By courtesy, a maid)
That always has a breaking heart,
Because she’s been betrayed.
The leading lady, as a rule,
Comes languidly along
To mention that she was a fool,
And went a little wrong;
Then staggers off, but soon returns
Her masher to upbraid—
And next the aged father learns
His girl has been betrayed.
Good Gracious ! how the story shocks
The old ’un —hear him rave !
It’s bound to bring his grizzled locks
With sorrow to the grave;
Such conduct fills him with disgust,
He screeches, “Worthless jade 1”
And curses (as we've often cuss’d)
The girl who’s been betrayed,
Or, if the “star ” be good and pure
And to the hero true,
Her foster-sister, to be sure,
Asks —“What am I to do?”
She wears a dark dramatic cloak,
She looks a bit afraid,
And groans, until we want to choke
The girl who’s been betrayed.
She tries to drown, in wadding streams,
Her misery and pain;
We brighten up, till someone seems
To fish her out again;
We fondly hope the tearful minx
Will die and go decayed—■
Alas ! she’s saved before she sinks,
That girl who’s been betrayed.
When villains throw her off a cliff
The “ funny man ” is there, _
And still she lives —in half a jiff
He grabs her by the hair ;
For ’tis her fate at last to come
Where wicked schemes are laid,
And strike the vile seducer dumb—
That girl who’s been betrayed.
She talks about her “ honour,” and
She speaks of “ ruin,” till
The simplest bard can understand
What makes her look so ill.
With playwrights she has been for long
A custom of the trade,
But O ! she likes to pitch it strong—
That girl who’s been betrayed.
Dear friends, that girl must disappear
From melodrama show;
She’s haunted us for many a year,
And now she’s got to go.
When next she starts her yarn forlorn,
Stand up—don’t be afraid,
And cry, “ Get out at once, begone !
We know you’ve been betrayed.”
EDMUND FISHER.
Satan and tho Statesman.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
11 Of this broad land the brain and hand
Am I,” the Statesman said,
“ Where would it be—l’d like to see—
If ’Enery Parkes were dead ? ”
Then Satan sly, who sat hard by
His ear, made answer free —
“ Old Sack of Chaff, you make me laugh !
Just where it is,” said he.
“ You have the marks, Sir Henry Parkes,
Of pride, from crest to spur :
I had ’em all—they caused my fall,
O Little Lucifer!
“ Up there on high I thought if I
Just sulk and go on strike
Why I can do what I dam—phew !
Beatified well like.
“ Mistake, no doubt—they slung me out,
With nothing to my name,
A poor plucked bird, and since, I’ve heard,
Heav’ngets on just the same.
“ And e’en in H —l if I should swell
Around, with haughty air,
And put on size, why, dash my eyes,
They’d fire me out of there !
“ So take my tip—keep down your lip—-
Though you were thrice a King—
Don’t put on airs, for no one cares
A dam for anything. ”
Sailing Song.
(FOR “OUR BEAUTIFUL HARBOUR.”)
Across the sky the white clouds fly,
The breeze begins to blow ;
If you must stay on land, good-bye !
A-sailing I must go ;
A-sailing I must go, sweetheart,
A-sailing I must go.
Then kiss me, dear, and dry that tear,
If you will bide behind,
A maiden waits on Manly pier,
She is not so unkind ;
She is not so unkind, sweetheart,
She is not so unkind.
But come with me, the wind blows free,
Tis time that we were gone !
By Middle Harbour grows a tree,
Your name is carved thereon ;
Your name is carved thereon, sweetheart,
Your name is carved thereon.
With wings of white our boat takes flight,
Her wake shines like a sword.
Ah, well may she go dancing light
That carries Love aboard !
That carries Love aboard, sweetheart,
That carries Love aboard !
The moments run till day is done,
Till day is done, too soon.
We went out sailing with the sun,
We’ll sail back with the moon.
We’ll sail back with the moon, sweetheart,
We’ll sail back with the moon.
V. J. D.
The Fate of a Missing Statesman.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The day is fast dying of a raging fever,
and slower and feebler become its gasps and its
throbs, until at length in one long-drawn breath,
which gently stirs the leaves, and for a single
moment fans the burning breast of Mother Earth,
it dozes off to rest. For a funeral-pall for
this latest born child of the sun, there is a filmy
gossamer of royal purple, with fine gold and
diamond dust, and for due solemnity nature
speaks only in whispers, the birds chirp under
their breath, the wearying monotone of the
cicada: is toned down to a diminuendo , and very
shortly all sounds are hushed, and all light—save
that of the stars—is gone.
* • •
Then the residents of the Australian
hamlet steal forth to fall into groups and talk
about the day that is gone, its joys and its
sorrows, its losses and its gains, but chiefly to
compare the readings of their various ther-
mometers. The resident constable and the pub-
lican lead the beau monde ; whilst the shearer
royally “ seeing through ” his cheque represents
Boheraianism, which looks with equal contempt
on all other sets, and ever regards itself —both in
town and in country—as the sole surviving
descendant of a long line of kings and usurps
regal prerogatives accordingly.
* • *
As the residents of a South Australian
hamlet were engaged talking over events and
swapping views one close evening, Senior-constable
Patrick Maher—who represented the Law of the
locality and bore on his own broad shoulders the
incubi of constable in charge, clerk of the
court, registrar of births, deaths and marriages ;
poundkeeper, dog - registrar, and inspector of
nuisances—was seated at his cottage-door—at the
rear of which stood the lookup —intently perusing
a week-old Adelaide paper. Seeing nothing that
either directly or remotely affected him in any
of his official capacities, he arose after awhile,
yawned, stretched his long legs, and cast his big
bottle-green eyes in the direction of the “ Golden
Fleece. ’ He had taken but three measured
regulation paces to the front, however, when he
suddenly stood to attention, and muttered
“blasht ’em !”
Whenever Constable Patrick Maher was dis-
pleased, his great face swelled and reddened, his
soulful eyes protruded from their sockets, and his
massive chest heaved and fell like an angry sea.
At such times, when the Majesty of the Law was
in the throes of labour, even his wife dared not
approach him.
The immediate cause of officer Maher’s dis-
pleasure on this occasion was the approach of two
men, bearing what the policeman’s practised eye
at once discerned to be a corpse. At once obey-
ing the constable’s peremptory order to carry the
body to the “shed beyant,” the two returned to
find Maher seated at a deal table, pen in hand,
ready to take down their story, which, shortly
told, was to the following effect.
They were commercial travellers, and when
within a mile or so of the village, had found
deceased lying on his face a short distance off the
main road, where, in their opinion, he had fallen
and perished from exhaustion. Constable Maher
made it a rule to administer a formal and compre-
hensive caution to anybody who came to impart
information to him in any of his official capacities,
and so, partly on general principles and partly,
perhaps, because the two men before him were,
like himself, in a somewhat “ fresh ” condition, he
performed his customary function on this occasion
with unusual solemnity, telling his two visitors,
by way of conclusion, that, being “ knowledgeable
min,” they ought to be aware that it was no part
of their duty as mere civilians to express any
opinion on the cause of death. That was for the
coroner to find out.
The two travellers were then bound over to
attend the inquest next day, and after the con-
stable had thrown some chaff-bags over the corpse
and locked the door of the shed which served as a
morgue, he wandered leisurely down to the tele-
graph-office to wire for the district coroner, who
lived ten miles away. This done, he went about
the task of collecting his jury, and for that pur-
pose repaired to the “Golden Fleece,” where,
during the evening, he had no difficulty in captur-
ing “ twelve good men and true ” in various
stages of intoxication, having meanwhile accepted
bribes in the form of drinks from many more for
the privilege of being “ let off.”
# * #
At ten o’clock on the following morning,
which happened to be St. Patrick’s Day, the
jury, many of them suffering a severe recovery,
were assembled in the parlour of the “ Golden
Fleece,” awaiting the arrival of the coroner, and
variously beguiling the time by sleeping, reflecting
and playing euchre for drinks. The only relief to
the monotony of the playing and drinking was
caused by an adipose and pompous German, who
happened unguardedly to propound the question
—“ Who vash dat St. Ba' rick, any vay ?” This
was sufficient to induce Con Dillon, another of
the intelligent twelve, to arise, take up three
holes in his waist-belt, spit on the floor, and
glower upon the speaker with rage and contempt.
“ And phwat the divil have ye to say about St.
Phadng, ye square-headed Yarman sausage?”
demanded the patriotic Con; “kirn out ye tub
o’ sour kraut, and Oi’ll teach ye about the glorious
sivinteenth of ould Oireland. Hooruo !”
What kind of a national celebration might have
ensued it is hard to say, had not Constable
Patrick Maher, in the full dignity of his office,
entered at that moment, and announced the
arrival of the coroner—a little, fat, bald ball of
humanity, who waddled in with the curt announce-
ment, “ Gentlemen, choose your foreman.” The
choice fell on the German, who was the soberest
of the party, not without a malignant scowl from
Con Dillon, who was the drunkest, by way of
protest.
The usual preliminaries having been arranged,
the doctor —whom the coroner had brought with
him —was requested to go down to the lock-up,
a distance of about a mile, and make a post-
mortem examination of the body while the Con-
stable’s evidence was being taken. Constable
Patrick Maher then, having kissed the Bible with
aloud report, straightened himself up, fixed his
ejes on vacancy, and bore testimony as follows,
in the sententious style peculiar to policemen,
watching the coroner’s pen the while to see that
he was not getting ahead of that fussy little
functionary : —“About tin p.m. on the aveningav
yesterday I was standing in front of me dhure —
at a distance, roughly spaking, of about foive
paces—and was nigligintly laning aginst a posht,
whin I was suddenly recalled to meself by the
appearance of two reshpectably-drissed men,
slightly under the influence of drink, bearing
betune them the corpse, which is lying beyant in
the lock-up. They enquired for a constable, and
I replied—which Oi am a saynyer wun—being in
plainclothes at the toime. The men described them-
selves as travellers, and deposed that they had
found deceased lying on his face, a moile or so out
of the village, I cautioned them in the usual
way, and bound them over ”
Just here the doctor burst into the room in a
stats of breathless excitement and red-hot fury,
and in answer to the Coroner, who said, “Well,
doctor, what do you make of it?” replied—banging
his hat on the table —
“ Make of it! I make it a d d wax-headed
dummy, belonging to some travelling show or
tailor’s shop, and if this mutton-headed constable
had taken the trouble to look at it, we might
have been spared a jaunt of ten miles in the dust
and heat.”
It then burst upon the dormant intellect of the
jury that at that moment there was an itinerant
wax-works exhibition, located in a barn a short
distance from the hotel, whence the voice of the
showman could even then be heard, inviting the
locals to “ roll up and witness the greatest collec-
tion of wax-figgers south of the line.”
The two commercial travellers when called upon
failed to answer to their names, and while the
inquest was proceeding two rakish-looking
objects, convulsed with mirth and with _ hats
pulled over their eyes, rapidly drove off behind a
pair of horses in a southerly direction. They
passed on their way a weird-looking creature of
dejected visage, in a pie-bald costume—who was
no other than the aforesaid showman—cursing and
perspiring over the task of nailing to a three-rail
fence a hand-written placard of which the follow-
ing is a copy :
WERE HAS last night the grand Hold man
Parkes was removed Out of His place in My
show and King solomon has his nose chipped off
without My Knowledge or consent, contrairey to
law a reward of arf a crown will be paid for the
person or persons who dun it.
God Save the queen.
By Order Jabez Waxman.
N.B. man and Money at the “ lucky dog ” Hotel
up the Road.
Within a month Patrick Maher was transferred
to another field of official usefulness, and it is
said that to this very day his successor never
takes over a corpse until he has probed it all over
with a cold-chisel.
HENRY O’DONNELL.
In a lecture on “ Municipal Socialism,”
Rev. Joseph Wood lately advocated the munici-
palisation of the tramways and electric-lighting,
and the establishment of free education, a public
gymnasium, a city band, and, above all things, the
purchase of an estate by corporations, on which
to build, as required, thousands of model work-
men’s dwellings, each provided with a garden and
the whole with public hall, free library, baths and
wash-houses, gymnasium, school, recreation-
ground and public garden—all communicating
with the centre of the town by a municipal tram-
way, a season-ticket for this tramway being
included in the rent.
Young Armytage, the little Victorian
M.L.A., "objects to the title “ Australian Com-
monwealth,” on the ground that there is “ nothing
distinctive in it at all.” The aspiring youth, after
careful enquiry into the matter, cannot discover
that the word expresses the relations existing
between himself (whose acres have come to him
with a double “ first family ” pedigree in the male
line) and the struggling “cockatoo” farmer with
no ancestry worth mentioning. This, however,
is merely the fault of private circumstances, and
in no way detracts from the distinctiveness of
“ Commonwealth ’as a political terra. Armytage
would prefer the word “Federation,” which is
certainly not distinctive, and would serve only as
a reminder that some An ralians- beg pardon—
are too well fed, whilst others go miserably short
of rations.
Prince Baldwin of Flanders, heir-pre-
sumptive to the Belgian crown, who died suddenly
a few months ago, has left the usual little story
behind him. The affair was hushed up at the
time, and it was vaguely rumoured that the
deceased had gone oft through a dozen different
ailments. He was seized with a mysterious pain
in his outside and had gangrene in the coat-tail
pocket —he had fallen up dead through corns in
the head—he had caught cold through being out
late at a meeting of the social purity society, and
he also fell and broke his neck coming from
church. But it has now leaked out that he went
the way of princes in general, and died through a
joyous spree and a woman. It was the old, old
story—he thought the husband was out for the
night, and the husband came home after all, and
pounded madly on the front door. Then the dis-
tracted prince looked out of the window, and the
man below saw him, and realised the situation,
and burst in, carrying the casement and all the
curtains along with him, and rushed up the stair
to wallop the intruder with the tongs. A dis-
tracted female went into hysterics among the bric-
a-brac on the sideboard, and leaped and gambolled
among the furniture generally. Then the prince
made a frantic effort to climb down a water-pipe,
but half way down his plentiful ear got hitched on
a nail, and his teeth caught on a projecting brick,
and he hung there howling and praying, with his
collar burst, and his hair standing up, and his hat
stove in, and all his shirt-buttons giving way.
The horrified lady inside grasped her enraged
husband and implored him not to bang the Lord’s
Anointed, and he asked sternly if it was the Lord’s
Anointed who was making the night hideous sus-
pended against the side of the house. Being in-
formed that it was, he got his gun, and then the
lady leaped in the air with a howl and fell in-
sensible under the grate. Outside, the Lord’s
Anointed was making a worse row than ever,
and swinging sadly up and down the wall. A
gun slowly emerged through the window, and the
prince shrieked out to the holder of it that he
would make him a duke on the spot provided he
didn’t shoot. There was no answer, only the gun
t came further out and a staring head came out
after it, and the heir apparent yelled that he
would make him three dukes —eighteen dukes —
would give him an estate—would lend him a
harem, ir he would—the gun went off just then,
and the billet of heir to the Belgian crown fell
vacant. This is the latest accout of how Bald-
win took his departure. We have added a few
interesting details to give thrill to the situation,
but otherwise it is correct up to the last available
advice.
Messrs. Hertzberg, Millingen and Co.
Brisbane.
The house of Messrs. Hertzberg, Millingen and
Co., General Merchants and Importers, Charlotte-
street, Brisbane, is able to supply most of the
wants of Queensland residents, not only with goods
of Australian make, but also in wares of European
and American manufacture. A glance through their
warehouse is sufficient to bespeak its importance.
A large, solid brick building of imposing appear-
ance, divided into five stories, it could not fail
to strike the eye of The Bulletin’s represen-
tative as one of the finest and most prosperous
importing houses in the city of Brisbane. Now,
a word as to the inception and origin of this im-
portant business establishment. Although started
but two and a half years ago, it has in the mean-
time met with pronounced success, and as it is
steadily advancing month by month in a safe and
profitable way its prospects are now well assured.
There are four partners in the business, all prac-
tical men and thoroughly conversant with the
various details of the departments which comes
under their immediate control. Mr. A. M.
Hertzberer, who has been in business in Queens-
land for 25 years, has charge of the office, counting-
house, and financial branch ; Mr. C. S. Millingen
(formerly with Messrs. Hoffnung and Co.) has the
management of the warehouse. Of the other
partners, Mr. B. Cohen—who, by the way, is
a son of Mr. S. Cohen, of Ulmarra, one of the
most respected residents of the Clarence district —
has charge of one of the important floors in the
establishment,, besides exercising a general super-
vision with Mr. Millengen. Mr. Abraham Hertz-
berg (brother of the senior partner) is the firm’s
chief travelling representative, and occupies a
forward place among the many industrious com-
mercial travellers of Queensland.
When the firm was organised early in 1888 it
was decided that Messrs. Hertzbergand Millingen
should visit Europe to make personal selection of
all goods required, and come in closer touch with
the merchants and manufacturers. One of the
most important features of the trip was securing
the sole agency for Queensland of the Victoria
Porcelain Works, Carlsbad, Bohemia, celebrated
for the beauty and delicacy of finish of its china
and glass fabrics. Messrs. H. M. and Co., in
accepting orders for special china designs, wish it
notified that, in addition to any particular design
selected, they can arrange to have photographs,
monograms, or inscriptions wrought into the
china according to the wishes of the purchaser.
A brief glance shows that one floor is devoted to
crockery, china, glassware, including all the most
useful, delicate and fanciful goods made by the
hand of man. Another floor contains a large
assortment of ironmongery of all descriptions, in-
cluding electro-plased ware, cutlery, American
hardware, guns, rifles, revolvers, large, strong
safes (by the Midland Safe Co., Birmingham),
also a very fine collection of English saddles and
saddlery goods. On another floor is on view
pianos, billiard-tables, Austrian bentwood furni-
ture, writing-cases, portmanteaux, travelling-bags,
brief-bags, and innumerable other goods. Next
comes the tobacconist and perfumery department,
to which is attached the Queensland agency for
David Dunlop’s Columbia and Derby Tobaccos
(an imnnnse success), Kinney Bro’s, Sweet
Caporal Cigarettes, Motley’s King of All Cut
Tobacco, Virginia, La Divinidad Cigars and
A tlantica Cigars. As to perfumery, the supply
is legion, including all the distillations and col-
lections of the choicest flowers. A special feature
of the perfumery department is the sole agency for
Queensland of Jas. S. Kirk and Co., Chicago,
prize brands toilette soaps, now new to the Aus-
tralian colonies. The stationery department con-
tains account books, ledgers, novels, &c., in al
bindings, writing-paper, and many other items.
The top floor is reserved for the toy depart-
ment-hobby-horses, jumping-jacks, toy games,
bagatelle boards, and every class of game.
On the basement floor, all goods are packed
and despatched to the order of various consti-
tuents. Messrs. Hertzberg, Millinger & Co.
employ thirty-two hands, whilst six commercial
travellers are doing business over the length and
breadth of Queensland. They have agencies at
New York, Paris, Vienna, Hamburg, Bohemia
and London.

The. Labourer.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The Laborer rises at the break of day,
To groan out life in toil for paltry pay—
Pay that scarce feeds and covers him with rags,
And, captive to the Rich, his chain’d soul drags
One woe of sweat, yet counting toil a boon,
He bends his patient back from dawn till noon.
At noon, by leave, he lights his sullen eye,
And curses God and man and earth and sky._
His grudged brief breathing-respite o’er, again
He bows his manhood to eventless strain,
Nor pauses, watched with rigorous intent,
Till, equally, the day and he are spent. *
So, drearily, his body is confined ;
But who can plumb the hell that’s in his mind ?
Perchance a thought of children, or of wife,
With feeble pallor glints his clouded life ;
Perchance his gloom with memory is fed
Of wife and child, by famine’s freezing, dead.
No errant beam of Hope illumes his face,
lie has no ties among the caste of race, _
He cannot claim to hold the humblest niche
Of common right—that privilege for the Rich,
Who, like to Judas, traitor to the Christ,
Still sell their Brother Man as may be priced.
Far better, thinks he, that he’d ne’er been born,
Yet finds defiance for the tyrant’s scorn.
The Rich may mock him as their flunkeys goad,
He bears their insults as he bears their load.
Few words of kindness ever greet his ears
Toil, toil, and toil, all through the weary years.
From him hides Hope; Woo mates him all his life ;
He’ll die of toil amid a devil’s strife,
Rich buy and sell him as they do ah ox; ,
Kings, for their pleasure, hunt him like a fox ;
His life one horror, and his death no knell,
He fears not God, nor gallows, nor a hell,
Nor spirit-torments parsons paint as rife —
Why should he fear ? He has his Hell in Life,
Where Privilege embrutes him in the dust,
For sake of gold, to pamper sordid lust;
That gold, wrung from his flesh, his blood, his
bones,
His manhood’s misery, his old-age’s groans.
All-worshipp’d gold, the Murderer of Toil I
Black-mail of Poverty, a bloody spoil!
As yet, the wakening masses only hum
The fragments of the anthem yet to come,
When, not for Justice will meek pliants sue,
sut Retribution on the gorging few
Who banish leisure from the Labourer’s life,
And draw their fatness out of social strife ;
Who grip the masses in a choking thrall,
And bid them show deep gratitude|for gall.
The time is near when all these bonds shall break,
When what the Rich won’t give, the Poor will
take;
When ’twill be deemed far better worth to die
Amid rebellion’s mad, unreasoning cry,
Than, for a space, to dodge a restful grave,
By living on, and dying still, a slave.
O, wise among the powers that tyrannise
(If there be any now among you wise ?),
Be warned in time. Let J ustice shape your plan;
Hear Labour now—as Man to Brother-Man.
JAMES BARR.
When the foundation-stone of the Eight
Hours’ memorial statue was laid in Melbourne,
Premier Munro made a speech about the grandeur
of Labour, and remarked that he had himself
toiled “in his shirt-sleeves” for 11J hours per
diem. He stopped there just when he was on
the brink of saying something interesting. What
the audience wanted to know was how he liked
it, and whether he was contented and happy in
those days, and if he ever said anything then
about the grandeur of Labour, and what show he
thought he might have had of becoming a Premier
if he had kept on working, instead of taking to
banking, money-lending, and land-booming, which
are not working in any reasonable sense, but
trading on other people’s work. Also, he might
have mentioned whether he ever knew any brawny
toiler who was enthusiastic about the beauty of
Labour, except after he had given up labouring.
For the unvarnished truth of the matter is that
Labour, in most of its aspects, is the saddest,
dreariest, dullest, most monotonous, and most
heart-breaking thing beneath the snn. If the
labourer is working on his own account,
and for his own sole profit, he may extract
pleasure out of the most unpromising intervals,
but the individual who breaks stones, or shovels
coal, or digs, or ploughs, or carries bricks
up a ladder, or does one of a thousand routine jobs
for an employer, has got an occupation that is as
melancholy and hopeless as that of a blind horse
in a coal mine. And as there is only room for about
one man in a hundred to quit this mode of life,
and become an employer, or a banker, or a land-
lord, for the simple reason that the other ninety-
nine are wanted for him to live upon when he has
joined that carnivorous trio, it follows that the
balance have very little amusement except drink,
and strikes, and things of that sort, and it is the
odd man who makes observations about the
pleasures of toil. And, as a rule, it is a matter of
mere accident who is that odd man out of a
hundred, and the man who is taken is very much
the same as the man who is left. It only wanted
a passing chance about the size of a hair and the
affable Premier with his oily platitudes about the
grandeur of Labour might have been a shearer
named Munro who got six months for using
violence to the police at Clermont.
Ode to the People.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Upon the cross of slavery
The people long have hung ;
The million toiling for the few,
The face of Justice hid from view,
E’en sturdy Truth has ages through
Withheld her clarion longue.
The golden tyrant, centuries gone,
Has triumphed over right;
Has taken for his dwelling-place
The birthright of the human race,
Bade Liberty to cloud her face,
And bow before his might.
The world before his loathsome spaw»
Has grovelled in the dust;
The blood of man has trickled down,
And woman of her fairest crown
Has been despoiled, to ’suage his frown,
Or sate his brutal lust.
And still beneath his “ noble ” feet
Man lays his manhood down ; ,
Consents his birthright to forego,
Owns his hereditary foe
As lord of all the earth below,
And cringes at his frown.
But see ! through yonder dark cloud’s rift
What gleam of light outsprings ?
Has Liberty awaked from death ?
The sword of Justice left its sheath ?
Has Truth renewed her wav’ring breath
And spread her snowy wings ?
Mankind ! uplift your prostrate form 1
Strike, and ye shall be free 1
The darkness has begun to part,
The rays of dawnlight onward dart,
Announcing to your fettered heart
The Morn of Liberty.
Oppression’s tree bears bitter fruit
That tyranny must taste;
Proclaim your manhood while ye may.
Welcome the dawn of that bright day
When Justice shall alone hold sway,
Ho more to he debased.
A.L.O.Z.

What folly it would be to out grass with a pair of scissors! Yet peo-
Sle do equally silly things every day. Modem progress has grown up
rom the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
for all scouring and house-cleaning.

Mr. W. H. Kent, J.P., a Successful
Colonist.
Mr. W. H. Kent is about the best-known layman
in Queensland, and in the city of Brisbane there
is no more popular man. He has been thoroughly
identified with Queensland for many years past,
and, independent of being recognised as one of
the ablest business men in the city, he is favour-
ably known in sporting circles, as also on the magis-
terial bench. Air. Kent is a native of Sydney,
and the second son of the late Captain
Charles Kent, of Stepney, London, an officer
who accompanied (his relative) Lord Castle-
reagh in that memorable tour through
Ireland which culminated, it was stated, in Lord
Castlereagh’s reports upon Irish affairs being as
prejudicial as they were untruthful. Fortunately
Captain Kent’s observation and sense of reason
with regard to the Irish question showed matters
in a very different aspect, and his able articles
in the columns of The Times dealing with the
subject were characterised by their impartiality,
vehemence and reason. W hen Lord Castlereagh
died, Captain Kent went on a tour to China,
America and finally arrived in Sydney, where
Mr. W. H. Kent was bom in 1836, his mother
dying the same week. She was the third daughter
of the Hon. Wheeler Barrington, of Merrion
Square, Dublin, her two sisters having been
attendants as Maids of Honor to Queen Victoria.
When Mr. W. H. Kent was but five years of age
his father had occasion to go to England and
afterwards sent to Sydney for his son. Soon
afterwards Captain Kent died, and his young son
returned to Sydney, his native place. Here Mr.
Kent obtained the rudiments of a useful educa-
tion at “ Smither’s ” and “ Gordon's ” schools,
institutions well-known in those days.
The discovery of the goldfields soon found young
Kent to the front, first at the Ophir, then the
Turon, thence to Braidwood and Adelong. He had
the good fortune to secure a blessing greater than
riches—a good wife—to whose counsel and advice
Mr. Kent is indebted for much of his present pro-
sperity Mr. Kent’s association with the gold-
fields was in a business capacity. He ran horse
and cattle sales-yards at one and the same time at
Adelong, Kiandra, Lambing Flat, and Forbes.
His energy was phenomenal his journeys
on horseback during a single day often tot-
ting up 80 to 90 miles. Asa horse sales-
man, it is often remarked that Mr. Kent
had no equal. Having got tired of a life on the
goldfields, he went to Brisbane, and there settled
m his old line for 20 years as a horse salesman, then
as a land speculator, as also a well-known and
popular breeder of thoroughbred stock, and has,
without doubt, done much to place the Queens-
land turf in the sound position it now occupies.
Mr. Kent acted as honorary secretary to the
Queensland Turf Club for a number of years and
put the club’s affairs in a sound and prosperous
condition, but not before he had spent some
thousands of pounds sterling in introducing
high - class thoroughbred stock. Mr. W. H.
Kent has owned probably more racehorses
during the last 36 years than any other man in
Australia, and some rare “ clinkers ” among them,
notably Le Grand, My Lord, “ The Australian
Peer,” Napper Tandy, Tippler, Fernando, Epi-
gram, and Darebin. Like most genuine sports-
men, Mr. Kent never bets, but runs his horses
for his own pleasure. Asa business man
Mr. Kent is known and appreciated among his
fellow-men as the acme of honour and good-
heartedness. He carries his years well when it is
remembered that his eldest son is a Doctor of
Medicine, another son a well-known solicitor,
and another a banker—this out of a family of ten.
Mr. Kent for the present takes no part in politics,
but is favourably known as a good and impressive
speaker. He is a large property-holder in Brisbane,
and is closely associated with many large financial
institutions. One of his most brilliant moves was
the establishment of the Royal Bank of Queens-
land, one of the most successful banking institu-
tions throughout Australia.

“THE BULLETIN."
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
** Th. © B ulle t i lx,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
It the only paper which has a consistent and tU$
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Governments
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prim
System.
A United Australia and Protection
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as unintereating more
than half of what appears in the newspapers at
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti*
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was’
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains>
and has become a vast property, because it pos.*
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, ita
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matte*
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-shelier of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official —that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than ant
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
